reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import torch
import torch.nn as nn

class Model(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, input_size, hidden_size, num_layers, output_size, dropout=0.0):
        """
        Initialize the LSTM model.

        :param input_size: The number of expected features in the input `x`
        :param hidden_size: The number of features in the hidden state `h`
        :param num_layers: Number of recurrent layers
        :param output_size: The number of output features
        :param dropout: If non-zero, introduces a Dropout layer on the outputs of each LSTM layer except the last layer, with dropout probability equal to `dropout`
        """
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        # Initialize hidden state with random values
        self.lstm = nn.LSTM(input_size, hidden_size, num_layers, batch_first=True, dropout=dropout, bidirectional=False)
        self.fc = nn.Linear(hidden_size, output_size)
    
    def forward(self, x, h0, c0):
        """
        Forward pass through the LSTM model.

        :param x: The input tensor, shape (batch_size, sequence_length, input_size)
        :return: The output tensor, shape (batch_size, sequence_length, output_size)
        """
        
        # Forward propagate LSTM
        out, state = self.lstm(x, (h0, c0))  # out: tensor of shape (batch_size, seq_length, hidden_size)
        
        # Decode the hidden state of the last time step
        out = self.fc(out[:, -1, :])  # out: tensor of shape (batch_size, output_size)
        
        return state[1]

# Test code
batch_size = 10
sequence_length = 512
input_size = 128
hidden_size = 256
num_layers = 6
output_size = 10
dropout = 0.0

def get_inputs():
    return [torch.rand(batch_size, sequence_length, input_size),torch.rand((num_layers, batch_size, hidden_size)),torch.rand((num_layers, batch_size, hidden_size))]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [input_size, hidden_size, num_layers, output_size, dropout]

Actually, let me think about this more carefully. If W_ih_T is (128, 1024), and we tile by rows (the input dimension), each tile has some rows and 1024 columns. For layer 1: W_ih needs 1.5 MB, W_hh needs 1 MB. Total 2.5 MB. Can we fit in registers? Each thread can use up to 255 registers. 256 threads * 255 registers = 65,280 registers per block. At 32 bits each = 256 KB. Not enough for 2.5 MB. What if we use tensor cores? The dimensions are too small for efficient tensor core usage. Let me reconsider. Maybe I should just write a simple kernel that doesn't try to be overly clever, and see how it compares to the reference. CuDNN has very high overhead for small problems. A fused kernel might win just by avoiding all the overhead of multiple kernel launches and workspace allocation. Actually, let me look at this from a different angle. The reference does: 1. `self.lstm(x, (h0, c0))` - a single cuDNN call 2. `self.fc(out[:, -1, :])` - a single linear layer call If cuDNN is already well-optimized, it might be hard to beat. Wait, but the problem says we need to run faster than the reference. Let me think about what could make our kernel faster: 1. **Skip dead computation**: Don't compute FC. Since we return c_n, not the FC output. This saves the ~2560 ops of the linear layer + the memory bandwidth of writing `out`. Actually, `out` is written by cuDNN anyway. But cuDNN might compute and write `out` even if we don't need it. In our custom kernel, for layers 1-5 we still need the full sequence output as intermediate. But for layer 6, we only need the final c state, not the full output! This means we can save: - Writing layer 6 output to global memory (10 * 512 * 256 * 4 = 5.2 MB) - The corresponding writes in cuDNN Wait, but for layers 1-5, we DO need the outputs to feed into the next layer. So we can't skip those. For layer 6 specifically, if we only need c_n (and h_n is part of the state), we don't need to write the output sequence. This saves 5.2 MB of memory bandwidth. Actually, h_n for layer 6 is also returned by nn.LSTM as state[0].state[1] is c_n. We need both h and c for all layers? Let's check the PyTorch LSTM documentation: - output: (batch, seq, hidden) - hidden states from LAST layer - h_n: (num_layers, batch, hidden) - final hidden states for EACH layer - c_n: (num_layers, batch, hidden) - final cell states for EACH layer So for the return value, we need c_n for all layers. And PyTorch computes h_n internally as part of the LSTM. For our custom kernel: - For layers 1-5: compute full output sequence (needed for next layer) - For layer 6: only compute final h and c, don't write output sequence This saves memory bandwidth but not computation. 2. **Fuse across layers?** If we process one time step across all layers before moving to the next time step, we could keep intermediate data in registers/shared memory. But this requires computing layer 1 step t, then layer 2 step t, etc. Layer 2 step t needs layer 1 step t's output. This is valid! We can process in a "wavefront" pattern. For each time step t and batch item b: - Compute layer 1 LSTM using x[b, t, :] and h1[b, t-1], c1[b, t-1] - Output h1[b, t] -> immediately used as input to layer 2 - Compute layer 2 LSTM using h1[b, t] and h2[b, t-1], c2[b, t-1] - ... - Compute layer 6 LSTM For one batch item, we process all layers for one time step before moving to the next. This has great data locality: - Layer k output at time t is consumed immediately by layer k+1 - No global memory write for intermediate layer outputs! - Only need to write layer 6 output? But we don't even need that! Wait, this is brilliant! For the return value (c_n for all layers), we don't need to write ANY intermediate outputs to global memory. We just need to keep h and c for each layer in registers/shared memory, process time steps sequentially, and at the end write out c for all layers. Memory for 6 layers of h and c: - 6 layers * 2 states (h, c) * 256 floats = 3072 floats per batch item - In registers: 3072 floats * 4 bytes = 12 KB per batch item - With 10 batch items total, if each block handles one batch item, 12 KB in registers is trivial This approach eliminates ALL intermediate output writes to global memory! The only HBM traffic is: - Read input x: 10 * 512 * 128 * 4 = 2.6 MB - Read weights: ~66 MB (but cached better) - Read initial h0, c0: 10 * 6 * 256 * 2 * 4 = 120 KB - Write final c: 10 * 6 * 256 * 4 = 60 KB Total memory traffic is dominated by weights + input. For the weights, each block reads all weights for all 6 layers. That's ~11.5M floats = 46 MB per block. With 10 blocks, if weights are in L2 cache, total HBM traffic is ~46 MB (not 460 MB!). Wait, can 10 blocks share weights via L2? Only if they run at different times or if all weights fit in L2. 46 MB > 50 MB L2 cache. Plus input, h0, c0... It might barely fit or might not. But if we launch one block at a time per SM (persistent kernel style), or if blocks for different batch items run sequentially, weights stay in L2. Actually, a simpler approach: launch 10 blocks (one per batch item), each processing all 6 layers. With 132 SMs, many SMs will be idle. But each block can process independently. For weight caching, if all 10 blocks run in parallel, they all read the same weights. With 64 blocks per SM and 132 SMs... only 10 blocks total, so most SMs are idle. For example: - 4 blocks per batch item: blocks process different chunks of the 1024 gates - 10 batch items * 4 blocks = 40 blocks total - Each block still needs all weights Or even better: for each batch item, use a 2D grid of blocks, one for each layer. But we need sequential processing across time and layers. Actually, the wavefront approach I mentioned above (all layers for one time step, per batch item) is natural for a single block per batch item. Let me stick with that. Algorithm for one block per batch item: ```cuda // In registers or shared memory float h[6][256]; float c[6][256]; // Initialize h, c from h0, c0 for layer = 0 to 5: for i = threadIdx.x to hidden_size-1 step blockDim.x: h[layer][i] = h0[layer, batch, i]; c[layer][i] = c0[layer, batch, i]; // For each time step for t = 0 to seq_len-1: float input[128]; // loaded from x[batch, t, :] for layer = 0 to 5: // Compute 4 gates * 256 = 1024 values // gates = W_ih * input + W_hh * h[layer] + bias // Apply sigmoid to i, f, o; tanh to g // c[layer] = f * c[layer] + i * g // h[layer] = o * tanh(c[layer]) // input for next layer = h[layer] ``` With 256 threads in a block, each thread can compute 4 gate values. For W_ih * input: - Thread 0: gates 0, 256, 512, 768 - Thread 1: gates 1, 257, 513, 769 - ... - Thread 255: gates 255, 511, 767, 1023 Each thread computes 4 dot products. For input size 128 or 256, each dot product is a loop. To make weight access coalesced, we need to store W in a specific format. If W_ih is (4H, input_size) in PyTorch (row major), and thread t accesses W_ih[k*256 + t, j] for j=0..input_size-1: - At j=0: thread t reads W_ih[k*256 + t, 0] at offset (k*256 + t) * input_size - Consecutive threads read offsets separated by input_size (128 or 256) - For input_size=128, threads 0..31 read offsets 0, 128, 256, ... which is strided by 128 floats = 512 bytes - A warp reads 32 cache lines! Very bad. To fix this, we need to store W in a transposed or blocked format. If we transpose W_ih to (input_size, 4H), thread t reads W_ih[j, k*256 + t] at offset j * 4H + k*256 + t. - At j=0, k=0: thread t reads offset t. Consecutive threads read consecutive floats. Perfect coalescing! - Each warp reads 128 consecutive bytes = 2 cache lines (assuming 64B cache lines). Wait, 32 consecutive floats = 128 bytes = 2 64-byte cache lines. Or with 128-byte cache lines on H100, it might be 1 cache line for 32 floats. Actually H100 has 128B L1 cache lines I believe. 32 floats = 128 bytes = 1 cache line. Perfect! So if we store W_ih as (input_size, 4H) and access W_ih[j, g] where g is the gate index, we get perfect coalescing for the gate dimension across threads. Similarly for W_hh: store as (hidden_size, 4H). Thread t accesses W_hh[j, k*256 + t] for coalescing. Great! So we need to transpose the PyTorch weights. Now, each thread computes: ```cuda for (int k = 0; k < 4; k++) { int gate_idx = k * 256 + threadIdx.x; float sum = bias_ih[gate_idx] + bias_hh[gate_idx]; for (int j = 0; j < input_size; j++) { sum += W_ih_T[j * 4H + gate_idx] * input[j]; } for (int j = 0; j < hidden_size; j++) { sum += W_hh_T[j * 4H + gate_idx] * h[j]; } gates[k] = sum; } ``` Wait, W_ih_T[j * 4H + gate_idx] for different j are separated by 4H = 1024 floats = 4096 bytes. That's 32 cache lines apart! Not good for caching. Actually, within the j loop, each thread accesses memory strided by 4096 bytes. The cache line is 128 bytes, so each access brings in 128/4 = 32 floats. But the thread only uses 1 float before jumping 4096 bytes. L1 cache hit rate would be terrible. Better to reorder the loops. Instead of having each thread loop over j for a fixed gate, we can have the thread block cooperatively load a tile of W_ih_T and input into shared memory. Actually, let me reconsider. The access pattern is: - Thread t accesses W_ih_T[0, gate_t], W_ih_T[1, gate_t], W_ih_T[2, gate_t], ... - Address: gate_t, gate_t + 4H, gate_t + 8H, gate_t + 12H, ... For thread t, each access is separated by 4H floats = 4096 bytes. A cache line is 128 bytes = 32 floats. So each access touches a different cache line. There are 32 cache lines between consecutive accesses of the same thread. For a warp of 32 threads, at j=0 they access 0, 1, 2, ..., 31. These are consecutive = 1 cache line. At j=1 they access 4H, 4H+1, ..., 4H+31. These are consecutive = 1 cache line. At j=2 they access 8H, 8H+1, ..., 8H+31. These are consecutive = 1 cache line. So each j iteration, the warp reads 1 cache line. For input_size=128, that's 128 cache lines. For 256 threads (8 warps), each j iteration reads 8 cache lines. This is actually not bad! The L1 cache can hold recently accessed cache lines. But with 128 cache lines per matmul and L1 cache being ~256 KB = 2048 cache lines, that's fine. The issue is that the next matmul (W_hh) uses different addresses. Actually, the bigger issue is that for 512 time steps, we do 512 * 6 = 3072 matmuls per batch item. Each matmul reads different W rows. Since W_ih is only 128 rows, we heavily reuse the same W_ih across time steps! The cache lines for W_ih_T[j, :] will be in L1/L2 after the first time step. Similarly for W_hh_T, we reuse the same weights across all 512 time steps. So the memory access pattern is actually very cache-friendly after the first time step! Let's think about caching more carefully: - W_ih_T for layer 1: 128 rows * 1024 cols * 4 bytes = 512 KB - W_hh_T for layer 1: 256 rows * 1024 cols * 4 bytes = 1 MB - Total per layer: 1.5 MB L1 cache per SM on H100: ~256 KB L2 cache: 50 MB Layer 1 weights don't fit in L1 but fit in L2. Across 10 blocks, if weights are read from HBM once and stay in L2, subsequent accesses are fast. For a single block processing 512 time steps, it accesses layer 1 weights 512 times. After the first few accesses, weights are in L2, so reads are fast. L2 bandwidth is ~10-20 TB/s on H100. Each matmul reads: - W_ih_T: 128 cache lines * 128 bytes = 16 KB (for 256 threads... wait, let me recalculate) Actually for 256 threads computing 1024 gates: - Each j iteration, 8 warps read 8 cache lines from W_ih_T - For j=0..127: 128 * 8 = 1024 cache lines = 128 KB - Plus 128 KB for W_hh_T (j=0..255: 256 * 8 / 2?) Wait, with 256 threads, at each j we have 8 warps. Each warp reads 1 cache line. So 8 cache lines per j. For W_ih_T with input_size=128: 128 * 8 = 1024 cache lines = 128 KB per matmul. For W_hh_T with hidden=256: 256 * 8 = 2048 cache lines = 256 KB per matmul. Total per time step: 384 KB. At L2 bandwidth of 15 TB/s: 384 KB / (15 TB/s) = 25.6 nanoseconds. For 512 time steps: 512 * 25.6 ns = 13.1 microseconds. Plus actual compute... Hmm, but L2 bandwidth is shared across all SMs. With only 10 blocks, we might get more than the average bandwidth. Actually, I think this custom kernel could be very competitive. The key wins are: 1. No intermediate output writes 2. All layers fused into one kernel for each batch item 3. Minimal memory traffic Let me write this kernel. Wait, I also need to handle the fact that PyTorch stores LSTM weights differently. PyTorch's LSTM parameters are: - `weight_ih_l[k]`: (4*hidden_size, input_size) for layer k - `weight_hh_l[k]`: (4*hidden_size, hidden_size) for layer k - `bias_ih_l[k]`, `bias_hh_l[k]`: (4*hidden_size,) The gate order in PyTorch is: input, forget, cell, output (i, f, g, o) where g is the cell candidate (often called c_tilde). Wait, PyTorch uses: `i = σ(W_{ii} x + b_{ii} + W_{hi} h + b_{hi})`, etc. The standard order is: i, f, g, o where: - c_t = f * c_{t-1} + i * g - h_t = o * tanh(c_t) And g = tanh(W_{ig} x + b_{ig} + W_{hg} h + b_{hg}) Wait, let me be more careful. PyTorch LSTM formula (from docs): ``` i_t = σ(W_{ii} x_t + b_{ii} + W_{hi} h_{t-1} + b_{hi}) f_t = σ(W_{if} x_t + b_{if} + W_{hf} h_{t-1} + b_{hf}) g_t = tanh(W_{ig} x_t + b_{ig} + W_{hg} h_{t-1} + b_{hg}) o_t = σ(W_{io} x_t + b_{io} + W_{ho} h_{t-1} + b_{ho}) c_t = f_t * c_{t-1} + i_t * g_t h_t = o_t * tanh(c_t) ``` The weight matrices are concatenated: - W_ih = [W_{ii}; W_{if}; W_{ig}; W_{io}] (4H rows) - W_hh = [W_{hi}; W_{hf}; W_{hg}; W_{ho}] (4H rows) So gates are ordered: i, f, g, o. For the transposed format: - W_ih_T = [W_{ii}^T, W_{if}^T, W_{ig}^T, W_{io}^T] (input_size rows, 4H cols) - W_hh_T = [W_{hi}^T, W_{hf}^T, W_{hg}^T, W_{ho}^T] (hidden_size rows, 4H cols) Each column corresponds to a gate, so column 0..H-1 = i, H..2H-1 = f, 2H..3H-1 = g, 3H..4H-1 = o. Now for the kernel, I need to load weights in a preprocessed format. Since we're using `load_inline`, we can do this preprocessing in Python when the model is initialized. Let's design the ModelNew class: ```python class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, input_size, hidden_size, num_layers, output_size, dropout=0.0): super().__init__() # ... same as reference # We still create the reference parameters to copy weights self.lstm = nn.LSTM(input_size, hidden_size, num_layers, batch_first=True, dropout=dropout, bidirectional=False) self.fc = nn.Linear(hidden_size, output_size) def forward(self, x, h0, c0): # Call custom CUDA kernel return custom_lstm_forward(x, h0, c0, self.weights_ih_t, self.weights_hh_t, self.bias_ih, self.bias_hh) ``` Wait, but we need to preserve the Model interface, including constructor arguments. We can create the nn.LSTM but not use it in forward. But then we'd have unused parameters. That's fine. Actually, for the custom kernel, we need to preprocess the weights. We can do this in `__init__`: ```python def __init__(self, ...): super().__init__() self.hidden_size = hidden_size self.num_layers = num_layers # Create reference modules to get properly initialized weights lstm = nn.LSTM(input_size, hidden_size, num_layers, batch_first=True, dropout=dropout) self.fc = nn.Linear(hidden_size, output_size) # Copy and transpose weights self.weights_ih_t = [] self.weights_hh_t = [] self.bias_ih = [] self.bias_hh = [] for layer in range(num_layers): in_size = input_size if layer == 0 else hidden_size w_ih = lstm.weight_ih_l[layer].data # (4H, in_size) w_hh = lstm.weight_hh_l[layer].data # (4H, H) # Transpose to (in_size, 4H) and (H, 4H) self.weights_ih_t.append(w_ih.t().contiguous().cuda()) self.weights_hh_t.append(w_hh.t().contiguous().cuda()) self.bias_ih.append(lstm.bias_ih_l[layer].data.cuda()) self.bias_hh.append(lstm.bias_hh_l[layer].data.cuda()) ``` Wait, but the weights need to be registered as parameters or buffers so they move with .cuda() and .to() calls. We can use nn.ParameterList or just register_parameter. Actually, on H100 everything will be CUDA. Let me just register them as buffers using `register_buffer`. For the CUDA kernel, since we're using `load_inline`, we need to write the C++ host function and CUDA kernel. CUDA kernel design: ```cuda __global__ void lstm_forward_kernel( const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ h0, const float* __restrict__ c0, float* __restrict__ c_out, const float* const* __restrict__ weights_ih_t, const float* const* __restrict__ weights_hh_t, const float* const* __restrict__ bias_ih, const float* const* __restrict__ bias_hh, int batch_size, int seq_len, int input_size, int hidden_size, int num_layers ) { int batch_idx = blockIdx.x; if (batch_idx >= batch_size) return; int tid = threadIdx.x; int num_threads = blockDim.x; // typically 256 // Local state for all layers // Use shared memory for h and c to allow sharing between threads extern __shared__ float shared_mem[]; float* h_shared = shared_mem; // num_layers * hidden_size float* c_shared = shared_mem + num_layers * hidden_size; // num_layers * hidden_size float* gates = c_shared + num_layers * hidden_size; // 4 * hidden_size (for current layer) float* input_shared = gates + 4 * hidden_size; // max(input_size, hidden_size) // Load initial h0 and c0 for (int layer = 0; layer < num_layers; layer++) { for (int i = tid; i < hidden_size; i += num_threads) { h_shared[layer * hidden_size + i] = h0[layer * batch_size * hidden_size + batch_idx * hidden_size + i]; c_shared[layer * hidden_size + i] = c0[layer * batch_size * hidden_size + batch_idx * hidden_size + i]; } } __syncthreads(); // Process each time step for (int t = 0; t < seq_len; t++) { // Load input x[batch_idx, t, :] for (int i = tid; i < input_size; i += num_threads) { input_shared[i] = x[batch_idx * seq_len * input_size + t * input_size + i]; } __syncthreads(); int layer_input_size = input_size; for (int layer = 0; layer < num_layers; layer++) { const float* W_ih = weights_ih_t[layer]; // (layer_input_size, 4*H) const float* W_hh = weights_hh_t[layer]; // (H, 4*H) const float* b_ih = bias_ih[layer]; // (4*H) const float* b_hh = bias_hh[layer]; // (4*H) float* h_prev = &h_shared[layer * hidden_size]; float* c_prev = &c_shared[layer * hidden_size]; // Compute gates: W_ih * input + W_hh * h + b // Each thread computes some gates for (int gate_chunk = tid; gate_chunk < 4 * hidden_size; gate_chunk += num_threads) { float sum = b_ih[gate_chunk] + b_hh[gate_chunk]; // W_ih dot product for (int j = 0; j < layer_input_size; j++) { sum += W_ih[j * 4 * hidden_size + gate_chunk] * input_shared[j]; } // W_hh dot product for (int j = 0; j < hidden_size; j++) { sum += W_hh[j * 4 * hidden_size + gate_chunk] * h_prev[j]; } gates[gate_chunk] = sum; } __syncthreads(); // Apply activations and update h, c // Thread 0..hidden_size-1 can process for (int i = tid; i < hidden_size; i += num_threads) { float i_gate = sigmoid(gates[i]); float f_gate = sigmoid(gates[hidden_size + i]); float g_gate = tanhf(gates[2 * hidden_size + i]); float o_gate = sigmoid(gates[3 * hidden_size + i]); float c_val = f_gate * c_prev[i] + i_gate * g_gate; float h_val = o_gate * tanhf(c_val); c_prev[i] = c_val; // Store h in input_shared for next layer input_shared[i] = h_val; // If last layer, also update h_shared? Actually we need h for next time step h_prev[i] = h_val; } __syncthreads(); layer_input_size = hidden_size; } } // Write out final c states for (int layer = 0; layer < num_layers; layer++) { for (int i = tid; i < hidden_size; i += num_threads) { c_out[layer * batch_size * hidden_size + batch_idx * hidden_size + i] = c_shared[layer * hidden_size + i]; } } } ``` Shared memory size needed: - h_shared: 6 * 256 = 1536 floats - c_shared: 6 * 256 = 1536 floats - gates: 4 * 256 = 1024 floats - input_shared: max(128, 256) = 256 floats Total: 4352 floats = 17,408 bytes = ~17 KB That's tiny! We can easily use shared memory for all of this. With 256 threads per block: - Each thread computes 4 gate values (1024 / 256 = 4) for the W_ih and W_hh dot products. - For each gate value, the thread does a dot product of length input_size or hidden_size. Wait, I realize the loop `for (int gate_chunk = tid; gate_chunk < 4 * hidden_size; gate_chunk += num_threads)` means thread tid computes gates tid, tid+256, tid+512, tid+768. Each gate is computed with two dot products. For input_size=128, each dot product is 128 iterations. 4 gates * 128 * 2 = 1024 FMAs per thread per layer per time step. For 6 layers, average input_size is ~220 (mostly 256). Let's say ~240 per layer average. Total FMAs per thread per time step = 6 * 4 * (128+256) / 2 ≈ 6 * 4 * 192 = 4608 FMAs. For 512 time steps: 4608 * 512 = 2.36M FMAs per thread. For 256 threads: 604M FMAs per block. At 67 TFLOPS: ~9 microseconds. But memory bandwidth is the bottleneck. Each dot product reads unrolled across memory. Actually, a critical optimization: the weights W_ih and W_hh are the same for all time steps. We can try to use `__ldg` (load through read-only cache/texture cache) for the weights. Or mark them as `__restrict__ const`. On modern GPUs, the compiler should do this automatically. Another optimization: unroll the dot product loops. For input_size=128 and hidden_size=256, we can fully unroll or partially unroll. Actually, with #pragma unroll, the compiler might be smart enough. Let me think about a different layout for W_ih to improve cache locality further. Current layout: W_ih_T is (input_size, 4H). Thread tid accesses W_ih_T[j, tid], W_ih_T[j, tid+256], W_ih_T[j, tid+512], W_ih_T[j, tid+768] for each j. Wait, the loop is: ```cuda for (int j = 0; j < layer_input_size; j++) { sum += W_ih[j * 4 * hidden_size + gate_chunk] * input_shared[j]; } ``` For a fixed gate_chunk, thread accesses W_ih[0, gate_chunk], W_ih[1, gate_chunk], W_ih[2, gate_chunk], etc. These are strided by 4H = 1024 floats = 4096 bytes. Cache line = 128 bytes. Each access touches a different cache line. Very bad for L1. Can we reorganize? What if we store W_ih in blocked format: - Block size: (input_size, block_size) where block_size = num_threads = 256 - So W_ih_blocked has shape (input_size, 4, 256) for 4*H gates - Or (input_size, 4H) but stored with 256 elements contiguous for the same j Actually, if we access W_ih[j, gate_chunk] for a fixed j and varying gate_chunk, consecutive threads access consecutive memory. But in our code, for a fixed gate_chunk, j varies. Alternative: swap the loop order. Instead of each thread doing a full dot product for its assigned gates, we can have threads work together. For example, with 256 threads: - Process W_ih one row at a time (one j value at a time) - For each j, all 256 threads read W_ih[j, 0..255] (coalesced), multiply with input_shared[j], and accumulate partial sums in registers - Then another 256 threads read W_ih[j, 256..511], etc. Actually, we only have 256 threads. For 1024 gates: - Approach 1: 256 threads, each handles 4 gates. Thread loops over j. - Memory: W_ih[j, gate_chunk] for fixed gate_chunk, varying j. Strided access. Bad. - Approach 2: 256 threads, each handles 1 gate group of 4 (wait, 1024/256 = 4). - Actually same as approach 1. - Approach 3: For each j, 1024 threads would be ideal. But we only have 256. - We can process in 4 passes per j: - Step 1: threads 0..255 read W_ih[j, 0..255], multiply with input[j], add to gates[0..255] - Step 2: threads 0..255 read W_ih[j, 256..511], multiply, add to gates[256..511] - etc. - Then `gates` is in shared memory, and each thread updates 1/256 of the gates for each j. - Total work per thread per j: 4 reads, 4 multiplies, 4 adds to shared memory. - For input_size=128: 128 * 4 = 512 operations per thread per layer - Plus similar for W_hh: 256 * 4 = 1024 operations - Total per layer: ~1536 operations - This is fewer operations than approach 1 (which was 1024 per layer) because we're spreading the dot product across threads! Wait, let me count more carefully. Approach 1 (current): - Each thread computes 4 gate outputs independently - Per gate: input_size multiplies and adds - Per thread: 4 * input_size FMAs - Total FMAs per block: 256 * 4 * input_size = 1024 * input_size (correct) Approach 3: - For each j, each thread updates 4 gate partial sums in shared memory - Per j: 4 FMAs per thread - Total FMAs per block: input_size * 4 * 256 = 1024 * input_size (same!) So same amount of compute. But the memory access pattern is better: - For each j, all threads read W_ih[j, k*256 + tid] for k=0,1,2,3 - Consecutive threads read consecutive memory. Coalesced! - Then they read input_shared[j] (broadcast) - And update gates[k*256 + tid] in shared memory This is much better for memory coalescing! And shared memory access pattern: - Thread tid reads gates[k*256 + tid], adds W_ih[j, k*256 + tid] * input[j] - Consecutive threads access consecutive shared memory. No bank conflicts! Let me redesign with this approach: ```cuda // Shared memory __shared__ float gates[4 * HIDDEN_SIZE_MAX]; // 1024 __shared__ float h_shared[NUM_LAYERS_MAX * HIDDEN_SIZE_MAX]; // 6*256=1536 __shared__ float c_shared[NUM_LAYERS_MAX * HIDDEN_SIZE_MAX]; // 1536 __shared__ float input_shared[MAX_INPUT_SIZE]; // 256 // For each layer for (int layer = 0; layer < num_layers; layer++) { int in_size = (layer == 0) ? input_size : hidden_size; // Initialize gates with bias for (int i = tid; i < 4 * hidden_size; i += num_threads) { gates[i] = bias_ih[layer][i] + bias_hh[layer][i]; } __syncthreads(); // W_ih * input for (int j = 0; j < in_size; j++) { float x_val = input_shared[j]; for (int k = 0; k < 4; k++) { int idx = k * hidden_size + tid; if (idx < 4 * hidden_size) { gates[idx] += weights_ih_t[layer][j * 4 * hidden_size + idx] * x_val; } } } __syncthreads(); // W_hh * h for (int j = 0; j < hidden_size; j++) { float h_val = h_shared[layer * hidden_size + j]; for (int k = 0; k < 4; k++) { int idx = k * hidden_size + tid; if (idx < 4 * hidden_size) { gates[idx] += weights_hh_t[layer][j * 4 * hidden_size + idx] * h_val; } } } __syncthreads(); // Apply activations for (int i = tid; i < hidden_size; i += num_threads) { float i_gate = sigmoid(gates[i]); float f_gate = sigmoid(gates[hidden_size + i]); float g_gate = tanhf(gates[2 * hidden_size + i]); float o_gate = sigmoid(gates[3 * hidden_size + i]); float c_val = f_gate * c_shared[layer * hidden_size + i] + i_gate * g_gate; float h_val = o_gate * tanhf(c_val); c_shared[layer * hidden_size + i] = c_val; h_shared[layer * hidden_size + i] = h_val; if (layer < num_layers - 1) { input_shared[i] = h_val; } } __syncthreads(); } ``` Wait, but in the W_ih and W_hh loops, each thread updates 4 gates per j. With 256 threads, all 1024 gates are updated. But the `for (int k = 0; k < 4; k++)` loop means each thread does 4 memory accesses per j. Wait, let me check. On Ampere and Hopper, shared memory has 32 banks, each 4 bytes wide (for 32-bit accesses). So 128 bytes map to 32 banks. Accessing gates[idx] where idx = tid: no bank conflict (thread tid accesses bank tid % 32). Accessing gates[idx] where idx = H + tid = 256 + tid: bank (256 + tid) % 32 = tid % 32. Same as before. We can override `load_state_dict` or `_apply` in ModelNew. Actually, the cleanest approach is: don't maintain a separate copy. Access the reference nn.LSTM's parameters directly, transposing on the fly using CUDA. But transpose is expensive. Wait, there's another option. We can keep our custom buffers, and override `load_state_dict` to reload custom buffers after the parent call: ```python def load_state_dict(self, state_dict, strict=True): super().load_state_dict(state_dict, strict=strict) self._transpose_weights() ``` But actually, `nn.Module.load_state_dict` already calls `self._apply` which propagates to parameters and buffers. The issue is that our custom buffers aren't in the state dict. Hmm, what if we don't use buffers and instead transpose in forward? It might not be too bad if we reuse the same workspace. Actually, for the fixed test case with input_size=128, hidden=256, num_layers=6, batch=10, seq=512, a transpose of weights takes: - W_ih for 6 layers: total ~3M floats - Transpose on GPU: ~0.01 ms This is negligible compared to the LSTM compute. So we can transpose on the fly every forward pass! But that means we need to handle the transpose in CUDA. We could pass weights in PyTorch format and have the CUDA kernel read them. But then memory access is strided and slow. Alternative: in Python, do a quick transpose and copy before calling the kernel: ```python w_ih_t = [w.t().contiguous() for w in self.lstm.parameters() if 'weight_ih' in w.name] ``` But we need to identify the right parameters. In PyTorch, they're `lstm.weight_ih_l0`, `lstm.weight_ih_l1`, etc. Actually, we can just regenerate the transposed weights in forward if we cache them: ```python if not hasattr(self, '_cached_weights') or self._weights_dirty: self._cached_weights = [...] self._weights_dirty = False ``` And set `_weights_dirty = True` in `load_state_dict` or when parameters change. For simplicity, let me just transpose in forward() using a small helper. The overhead is minimal. Wait, actually there might be an even easier way. What if we just extract the pointer to the PyTorch tensor data and let the CUDA kernel read the weights directly? We can transpose the access pattern in the kernel. If W_ih is stored as (4H, input_size) by PyTorch, then element [g, j] is at g * input_size + j. In the kernel, if thread tid computes gate g and loops over j: ```cuda for (int j = 0; j < input_size; j++) { sum += W_ih[g * input_size + j] * input[j]; } ``` For consecutive threads computing gates g and g+1: - Thread g reads W_ih[g * input_size + 0], W_ih[g * input_size + 1], ... - Thread g+1 reads W_ih[(g+1) * input_size + 0], W_ih[(g+1) * input_size + 1], ... - At j=0: threads read g*input_size, (g+1)*input_size, ... - These are separated by input_size floats = input_size * 4 bytes - For input_size=128: 512 bytes apart - A warp of 32 threads reads 32 elements separated by 512 bytes = 16,384 bytes span - With 128-byte cache lines, that's 128 cache lines! Terrible. But we can restructure: have all threads work on the same gate g for the same j. Actually, this is what "approach 3" above does, but with transposed weights. Without transposing: ```cuda for (int j = 0; j < input_size; j++) { float x_val = input[j]; for (int k = 0; k < 4; k++) { int gate_idx = k * hidden_size + tid; gates[gate_idx] += W_ih[gate_idx * input_size + j] * x_val; } } ``` W access: W_ih[gate_idx * input_size + j] = W_ih[(k*H + tid) * input_size + j] For fixed j, consecutive threads (varying tid) access: W_ih[k*H*input_size + tid*input_size + j] = base + tid * input_size + j These are separated by input_size floats = input_size * 4 bytes. For input_size=128: 512 bytes apart. Same terrible striding. So without transposed weights, memory access is bad. We really need to transpose. Conclusion: transpose weights in Python before kernel launch. The overhead is small. Let me write the kernel now. For simplicity, I'll keep the kernel somewhat generic but optimized for the test case. CUDA kernel: ```cuda #include <cuda_fp16.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> __device__ __forceinline__ float sigmoid(float x) { return 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-x)); } __device__ __forceinline__ float tanh_approx(float x) { return tanhf(x); } __global__ void lstm_forward_kernel( const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ h0, const float* __restrict__ c0, float* __restrict__ c_out, const float* const* __restrict__ w_ih_list, const float* const* __restrict__ w_hh_list, const float* const* __restrict__ b_ih_list, const float* const* __restrict__ b_hh_list, int batch_size, int seq_len, int input_size, int hidden_size, int num_layers ) { int batch_idx = blockIdx.x; if (batch_idx >= batch_size) return; int tid = threadIdx.x; int num_threads = blockDim.x; extern __shared__ float smem[]; // Layout: h_storage, c_storage, gates, input_temp float* h_storage = smem; float* c_storage = h_storage + num_layers * hidden_size; float* gates = c_storage + num_layers * hidden_size; float* input_temp = gates + 4 * hidden_size; // Load initial states for (int layer = 0; layer < num_layers; layer++) { int layer_offset = layer * hidden_size; int global_offset = layer * batch_size * hidden_size + batch_idx * hidden_size; for (int i = tid; i < hidden_size; i += num_threads) { h_storage[layer_offset + i] = h0[global_offset + i]; c_storage[layer_offset + i] = c0[global_offset + i]; } } __syncthreads(); for (int t = 0; t < seq_len; t++) { // Load input for (int i = tid; i < input_size; i += num_threads) { input_temp[i] = x[batch_idx * seq_len * input_size + t * input_size + i]; } __syncthreads(); int layer_input_size = input_size; for (int layer = 0; layer < num_layers; layer++) { const float* w_ih = w_ih_list[layer]; // (layer_input_size, 4*hidden_size) const float* w_hh = w_hh_list[layer]; // (hidden_size, 4*hidden_size) const float* b_ih = b_ih_list[layer]; const float* b_hh = b_hh_list[layer]; // Initialize gates with bias for (int i = tid; i < 4 * hidden_size; i += num_threads) { gates[i] = b_ih[i] + b_hh[i]; } __syncthreads(); // W_ih * input for (int j = 0; j < layer_input_size; j++) { float x_val = input_temp[j]; // Each thread updates 4 gate values (one from each gate group) for (int k = 0; k < 4; k++) { int gate_idx = k * hidden_size + tid; gates[gate_idx] += w_ih[j * 4 * hidden_size + gate_idx] * x_val; } } __syncthreads(); // W_hh * h for (int j = 0; j < hidden_size; j++) { float h_val = h_storage[layer * hidden_size + j]; for (int k = 0; k < 4; k++) { int gate_idx = k * hidden_size + tid; gates[gate_idx] += w_hh[j * 4 * hidden_size + gate_idx] * h_val; } } __syncthreads(); // Apply activations and update states float* h_layer = h_storage + layer * hidden_size; float* c_layer = c_storage + layer * hidden_size; for (int i = tid; i < hidden_size; i += num_threads) { float i_gate = sigmoid(gates[i]); float f_gate = sigmoid(gates[hidden_size + i]); float g_gate = tanh_approx(gates[2 * hidden_size + i]); float o_gate = sigmoid(gates[3 * hidden_size + i]); float c_new = f_gate * c_layer[i] + i_gate * g_gate; float h_new = o_gate * tanh_approx(c_new); c_layer[i] = c_new; h_layer[i] = h_new; // Set input for next layer if (layer < num_layers - 1) { input_temp[i] = h_new; } } __syncthreads(); layer_input_size = hidden_size; } } // Write final cell states for (int layer = 0; layer < num_layers; layer++) { int global_offset = layer * batch_size * hidden_size + batch_idx * hidden_size; for (int i = tid; i < hidden_size; i += num_threads) { c_out[global_offset + i] = c_storage[layer * hidden_size + i]; } } } ``` Wait, I just realized a critical issue. In the W_ih and W_hh accumulation loops: ```cuda for (int k = 0; k < 4; k++) { int gate_idx = k * hidden_size + tid; gates[gate_idx] += w_ih[j * 4 * hidden_size + gate_idx] * x_val; } ``` `w_ih[j * 4 * hidden_size + gate_idx]` where gate_idx = k*H + tid. For fixed j and k, consecutive threads access w_ih[j*4H + k*H + 0], w_ih[j*4H + k*H + 1], etc. These ARE consecutive in memory! Great. But `gates[gate_idx]` accesses gates[k*H + tid]. For k=0: gates[0..255], consecutive threads access consecutive shared memory. No bank conflicts. However, ALL threads are reading `x_val = input_temp[j]` simultaneously. This is a broadcast, which is efficient in shared memory. There's one issue: the `gates[gate_idx] += ...` involves a read-modify-write on shared memory. Since each thread accesses a unique location, there's no race condition. But shared memory RMW has some latency. With 256 threads doing this simultaneously for 4 gate groups and layer_input_size iterations, it's a lot of shared memory traffic. Let me count shared memory accesses for layer 1: - W_ih loop: 128 * 4 = 512 RMWs per thread - W_hh loop: 256 * 4 = 1024 RMWs per thread - Total: 1536 RMWs per thread per layer - For 6 layers average ~220: ~1320 RMWs per thread per time step - For 512 time steps: ~675K RMWs per thread - 256 threads: 173M RMWs total - At maybe ~20 cycles per RMW: ~3.5B cycles. At 2 GHz: ~1.75 seconds. That can't be right. Wait, shared memory RMW is very fast, maybe 2-3 cycles. And they're pipelined. Plus the warps execute in parallel. Let me think differently. Each warp does 32 RMWs in parallel (one per thread). With 8 warps, that's 256 RMWs per cycle (approximately, if not memory-limited). At 2 GHz, 173M RMWs would take less than 1 ms. Actually, I think the bigger issue is that for each j, all threads load w_ih and gates. The w_ih loads are from global memory (or L2/L1 cache). Let me count global memory transactions. For w_ih[j * 4H + k * H + tid] with j, k fixed and tid=0..255: - 256 threads read 256 consecutive floats = 1024 bytes - This is 8 L1 cache lines (128 bytes each) or 32 L2 cache segments - One memory transaction serves the whole warp for consecutive access For K=0..3 and J=0..input_size-1: - Total unique memory accessed: input_size * 4 * hidden_size floats - For layer 1: 128 * 1024 = 131,072 floats = 512 KB - Number of cache lines: 512 KB / 128 B = 4096 cache lines - For 256 threads (8 warps), each j*k iteration, all warps read the same cache lines? No, each warp reads different data. Wait, for a fixed j and k, the 256 floats span 1024 bytes = 8 cache lines. With 8 warps, each warp reads a portion. But since 256 threads = 8 warps and the data is 8 cache lines, each warp might read one cache line (32 floats per warp = 128 bytes = 1 cache line). Actually, for coalesced access of 256 consecutive floats by 256 threads: - Warp 0 (threads 0-31) reads floats 0-31 = 128 bytes = 1 cache line - Warp 1 (threads 32-63) reads floats 32-63 = 1 cache line - ... - Warp 7 reads floats 224-255 = 1 cache line - Total: 8 cache lines per (j, k) For layer 1 W_ih: 128 * 4 * 8 = 4096 cache lines = 512 KB. Correct. For W_hh: 256 * 4 * 8 = 8192 cache lines = 1 MB. Correct. But I think my L2 bandwidth estimate is conservative. Also, cache lines are prefetched. Wait, I think my analysis is fundamentally flawed because I'm not accounting for the fact that weights are read from L2, and L2 bandwidth is much higher when all SMs hit the same cache lines ( broadcasts to multiple SMs). If my custom kernel takes 6.8 ms, it's much slower. But wait, that calculation assumed 640K cache lines * 128 bytes = 81.9 GB of L2 reads. But the total unique weight data is only ~12 MB. By processing time step t across all layers before moving to t+1, we never write intermediate outputs. This saves all the intermediate output memory traffic. Let me now write the full Python code for ModelNew. I'll use `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline`. Here's my plan: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cuda_source = r''' #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <cuda_fp16.h> __device__ __forceinline__ float sigmoid(float x) { return 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-x)); } __global__ void lstm_forward_kernel( const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ h0, const float* __restrict__ c0, float* __restrict__ c_out, const float* const* __restrict__ w_ih_list, const float* const* __restrict__ w_hh_list, const float* const* __restrict__ b_ih_list, const float* const* __restrict__ b_hh_list, int batch_size, int seq_len, int input_size, int hidden_size, int num_layers ) { int batch_idx = blockIdx.x; if (batch_idx >= batch_size) return; int tid = threadIdx.x; int num_threads = blockDim.x; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* h_storage = smem; float* c_storage = smem + num_layers * hidden_size; float* gates = c_storage + num_layers * hidden_size; float* input_temp = gates + 4 * hidden_size; // Load initial states for (int layer = 0; layer < num_layers; layer++) { int loff = layer * hidden_size; int goff = layer * batch_size * hidden_size + batch_idx * hidden_size; for (int i = tid; i < hidden_size; i += num_threads) { h_storage[loff + i] = h0[goff + i]; c_storage[loff + i] = c0[goff + i]; } } __syncthreads(); for (int t = 0; t < seq_len; t++) { for (int i = tid; i < input_size; i += num_threads) { input_temp[i] = x[batch_idx * seq_len * input_size + t * input_size + i]; } __syncthreads(); int in_size = input_size; for (int layer = 0; layer < num_layers; layer++) { const float* w_ih = w_ih_list[layer]; const float* w_hh = w_hh_list[layer]; const float* b_ih = b_ih_list[layer]; const float* b_hh = b_hh_list[layer]; for (int i = tid; i < 4 * hidden_size; i += num_threads) { gates[i] = b_ih[i] + b_hh[i]; } __syncthreads(); for (int j = 0; j < in_size; j++) { float xv = input_temp[j]; #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < 4; k++) { int gid = k * hidden_size + tid; gates[gid] += w_ih[j * 4 * hidden_size + gid] * xv; } } __syncthreads(); for (int j = 0; j < hidden_size; j++) { float hv = h_storage[layer * hidden_size + j]; #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < 4; k++) { int gid = k * hidden_size + tid; gates[gid] += w_hh[j * 4 * hidden_size + gid] * hv; } } __syncthreads(); float* h_layer = h_storage + layer * hidden_size; float* c_layer = c_storage + layer * hidden_size; for (int i = tid; i < hidden_size; i += num_threads) { float gi = sigmoid(gates[i]); float gf = sigmoid(gates[hidden_size + i]); float gg = tanhf(gates[2 * hidden_size + i]); float go = sigmoid(gates[3 * hidden_size + i]); float c_new = gf * c_layer[i] + gi * gg; float h_new = go * tanhf(c_new); c_layer[i] = c_new; h_layer[i] = h_new; if (layer < num_layers - 1) { input_temp[i] = h_new; } } __syncthreads(); in_size = hidden_size; } } for (int layer = 0; layer < num_layers; layer++) { int goff = layer * batch_size * hidden_size + batch_idx * hidden_size; for (int i = tid; i < hidden_size; i += num_threads) { c_out[goff + i] = c_storage[layer * hidden_size + i]; } } } ''' cpp_source = r''' torch::Tensor lstm_forward_cuda( torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor h0, torch::Tensor c0, std::vector<torch::Tensor> w_ih_list, std::vector<torch::Tensor> w_hh_list, std::vector<torch::Tensor> b_ih_list, std::vector<torch::Tensor> b_hh_list ) { int batch_size = x.size(0); int seq_len = x.size(1); int input_size = x.size(2); int num_layers = h0.size(0); int hidden_size = h0.size(2); auto c_out = torch::empty_like(c0); // Create device arrays for weight pointers std::vector<const float*> w_ih_ptrs, w_hh_ptrs, b_ih_ptrs, b_hh_ptrs; for (int i = 0; i < num_layers; i++) { w_ih_ptrs.push_back(w_ih_list[i].data_ptr<float>()); w_hh_ptrs.push_back(w_hh_list[i].data_ptr<float>()); b_ih_ptrs.push_back(b_ih_list[i].data_ptr<float>()); b_hh_ptrs.push_back(b_hh_list[i].data_ptr<float>()); } // Copy pointers to device auto w_ih_dev = torch::from_blob(w_ih_ptrs.data(), {num_layers}, torch::kCPU).to(x.device()); auto w_hh_dev = torch::from_blob(w_hh_ptrs.data(), {num_layers}, torch::kCPU).to(x.device()); auto b_ih_dev = torch::from_blob(b_ih_ptrs.data(), {num_layers}, torch::kCPU).to(x.device()); auto b_hh_dev = torch::from_blob(b_hh_ptrs.data(), {num_layers}, torch::kCPU).to(x.device()); int threads = 256; int blocks = batch_size; size_t smem_size = (2 * num_layers * hidden_size + 4 * hidden_size + std::max(input_size, hidden_size)) * sizeof(float); lstm_forward_kernel<<<blocks, threads, smem_size>>>( x.data_ptr<float>(), h0.data_ptr<float>(), c0.data_ptr<float>(), c_out.data_ptr<float>(), w_ih_dev.data_ptr<const float*>(), w_hh_dev.data_ptr<const float*>(), b_ih_dev.data_ptr<const float*>(), b_hh_dev.data_ptr<const float*>(), batch_size, seq_len, input_size, hidden_size, num_layers ); return c_out; } ''' # Load inline lstm_forward_cuda = load_inline( name='lstm_forward', cpp_sources=cpp_source, cuda_sources=cuda_source, functions=['lstm_forward_cuda'], extra_cuda_cflags=['-O3'], verbose=False ) class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, input_size, hidden_size, num_layers, output_size, dropout=0.0): super().__init__() self.input_size = input_size self.hidden_size = hidden_size self.num_layers = num_layers self.output_size = output_size # Reference modules (for weight init and potential state dict compat) self.lstm = nn.LSTM(input_size, hidden_size, num_layers, batch_first=True, dropout=dropout, bidirectional=False) self.fc = nn.Linear(hidden_size, output_size) def forward(self, x, h0, c0): # Transpose weights for better memory access w_ih_t = [] w_hh_t = [] b_ih = [] b_hh = [] device = x.device for layer in range(self.num_layers): w_ih = self.lstm.weight_ih_l[layer] # (4H, in_size) w_hh = self.lstm.weight_hh_l[layer] # (4H, H) b_ih_t = self.lstm.bias_ih_l[layer] # (4H) b_hh_t = self.lstm.bias_hh_l[layer] # (4H) # Transpose to (in_size, 4H) and (H, 4H) w_ih_t.append(w_ih.t().contiguous().to(device)) w_hh_t.append(w_hh.t().contiguous().to(device)) b_ih.append(b_ih_t.to(device)) b_hh.append(b_hh_t.to(device)) return lstm_forward_cuda.lstm_forward_cuda(x, h0, c0, w_ih_t, w_hh_t, b_ih, b_hh) ``` Wait, there are several issues: 1. `load_inline` signature: `name`, `cpp_sources`, `cuda_sources`, `functions`, etc. But `cpp_sources` should not contain the function definition if `functions` is used? Let me also think about what happens if we reduce the number of `__syncthreads()` calls. In our kernel: 1. After loading initial states: 1 sync 2. Per time step: - After loading input: 1 sync - Initialize gates: 1 sync - W_ih loop: 1 sync - W_hh loop: 1 sync - Activations: 1 sync Total per time step: 5 syncs * 512 = 2560 syncs `__syncthreads()` is relatively fast when all threads reach it, but 2560 syncs total for 256 threads * 10 blocks is a lot. Can we reduce syncs? - Loading input: need sync before using input_temp - Gate init: need sync before W_ih updates (all gates must be initialized) - W_ih updates: need sync before W_hh updates (all gate partials from W_ih must be done) - W_hh updates: need sync before activations - Activations: need sync before next layer uses input_temp or next time step Actually, some of these might be eliminable: - Gate init and W_ih updates can be merged: initialize in registers, not shared memory, then write to shared memory after W_ih? No, because W_ih needs to accumulate across threads, so gates must be in shared memory. - Wait, if each gate is updated by EXACTLY ONE thread (tid computes gate k*H + tid), then we don't need atomics. But we still need all W_ih contributions to be visible before W_hh contributions, and all contributions visible before activations. So the syncs are mostly necessary. Actually, here's an idea: use double-buffering for gates. Compute W_ih into gates_buffer1 while initializing gates_buffer2. But that doesn't really reduce syncs. Another idea: merge W_ih and W_hh. I'll keep it relatively simple and check correctness first. Actually, I just realized I should double-check the tolerance. fp32 uses atol=rtol=1e-4. That's quite loose. Let me finalize the code: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline CUDA_SOURCE = r''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <vector> __device__ __forceinline__ float sigmoidf_custom(float x) { return 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-x)); } __global__ void lstm_forward_kernel( const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ h0, const float* __restrict__ c0, float* __restrict__ c_out, const float* const* __restrict__ w_ih_ptrs, const float* const* __restrict__ w_hh_ptrs, const float* const* __restrict__ b_ih_ptrs, const float* const* __restrict__ b_hh_ptrs, int batch_size, int seq_len, int input_size, int hidden_size, int num_layers ) { int batch_idx = blockIdx.x; if (batch_idx >= batch_size) return; int tid = threadIdx.x; int num_threads = blockDim.x; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* h_s = smem; float* c_s = h_s + num_layers * hidden_size; float* gates = c_s + num_layers * hidden_size; float* input_s = gates + 4 * hidden_size; // Load initial h and c for (int layer = 0; layer < num_layers; layer++) { int loff = layer * hidden_size; int goff = layer * batch_size * hidden_size + batch_idx * hidden_size; for (int i = tid; i < hidden_size; i += num_threads) { h_s[loff + i] = h0[goff + i]; c_s[loff + i] = c0[goff + i]; } } __syncthreads(); for (int t = 0; t < seq_len; t++) { // Load input for this time step for (int i = tid; i < input_size; i += num_threads) { input_s[i] = x[batch_idx * seq_len * input_size + t * input_size + i]; } __syncthreads(); int cur_in_size = input_size; for (int layer = 0; layer < num_layers; layer++) { const float* w_ih = w_ih_ptrs[layer]; const float* w_hh = w_hh_ptrs[layer]; const float* b_ih = b_ih_ptrs[layer]; const float* b_hh = b_hh_ptrs[layer]; // Initialize gates with bias for (int i = tid; i < 4 * hidden_size; i += num_threads) { gates[i] = b_ih[i] + b_hh[i]; } __syncthreads(); // Accumulate W_ih * input for (int j = 0; j < cur_in_size; j++) { float x_val = input_s[j]; #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < 4; k++) { int gid = k * hidden_size + tid; gates[gid] += w_ih[j * 4 * hidden_size + gid] * x_val; } } __syncthreads(); // Accumulate W_hh * h for (int j = 0; j < hidden_size; j++) { float h_val = h_s[layer * hidden_size + j]; #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < 4; k++) { int gid = k * hidden_size + tid; gates[gid] += w_hh[j * 4 * hidden_size + gid] * h_val; } } __syncthreads(); // Apply activations float* h_layer = h_s + layer * hidden_size; float* c_layer = c_s + layer * hidden_size; for (int i = tid; i < hidden_size; i += num_threads) { float i_gate = sigmoidf_custom(gates[i]); float f_gate = sigmoidf_custom(gates[hidden_size + i]); float g_gate = tanhf(gates[2 * hidden_size + i]); float o_gate = sigmoidf_custom(gates[3 * hidden_size + i]); float c_new = f_gate * c_layer[i] + i_gate * g_gate; float h_new = o_gate * tanhf(c_new); c_layer[i] = c_new; h_layer[i] = h_new; if (layer < num_layers - 1) { input_s[i] = h_new; } } __syncthreads(); cur_in_size = hidden_size; } } // Write final c states for (int layer = 0; layer < num_layers; layer++) { int goff = layer * batch_size * hidden_size + batch_idx * hidden_size; for (int i = tid; i < hidden_size; i += num_threads) { c_out[goff + i] = c_s[layer * hidden_size + i]; } } } ''' CPP_SOURCE = r''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <vector> torch::Tensor lstm_forward_cuda( torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor h0, torch::Tensor c0, std::vector<torch::Tensor> w_ih_list, std::vector<torch::Tensor> w_hh_list, std::vector<torch::Tensor> b_ih_list, std::vector<torch::Tensor> b_hh_list ) { int batch_size = x.size(0); This creates a 1D tensor of `num_layers` floats. But `w_ih_ptrs` contains `const float*` pointers, which are 64-bit on a 64-bit system. Creating a float tensor from pointer data will truncate the pointers! This is a critical bug. `const float*` is 8 bytes, but `float` is 4 bytes. We need to use a data type that's the same size as a pointer. We can use `torch::kInt64` or `torch::kLong`. On most systems, `long` or `int64_t` is 8 bytes. ```cpp auto w_ih_dev = torch::from_blob(w_ih_ptrs.data(), {num_layers}, torch::kInt64).to(x.device()); ``` But then in the kernel, we need to cast it back: ```cuda const float* const* __restrict__ w_ih_ptrs ``` Wait, the kernel expects `const float* const*`, which is a pointer to an array of `const float*`. If we pass a `int64_t*` (or `long*`) device pointer, the CUDA compiler should interpret the memory correctly as pointers if we cast it. In C++: ```cpp lstm_forward_kernel<<<...>>>( ..., (const float* const*)w_ih_dev.data_ptr<int64_t>(), ... ); ``` Or we can declare the kernel parameter as `const int64_t*` and cast inside. But the kernel parameter should match. Actually, the simplest way is to use `void**` or `int64_t*` and cast inside the kernel. Actually, I just realized: when using `load_inline` with both `cpp_sources` and `cuda_sources`, the standard pattern is to put C++ host code (including pybind11 module or wrapper functions) in `cpp_sources`, and CUDA device code (kernels) in `cuda_sources`. The `cpp_sources` file can declare the kernels as `void kernel_name(...);` and then call them with `<<<>>>` if the `cpp_sources` file has a `.cu` extension or is compiled with nvcc. But `load_inline` always writes `cpp_sources` to a `.cpp` file, which is compiled with the host C++ compiler. Host C++ compiler can't compile `<<<>>>`. So the function that launches the kernel MUST be in `cuda_sources`. Here's a working pattern: ```python cpp_sources = ''' #include <torch/extension.h> torch::Tensor lstm_forward_cuda(torch::Tensor x, ...); ''' cuda_sources = ''' #include <torch/extension.h> __global__ void kernel(...) { ... } torch::Tensor lstm_forward_cuda(torch::Tensor x, ...) { kernel<<<...>>>(...); return ...; } ''' ``` The `cpp_sources` forward-declares the function, and the auto-generated pybind11 code includes `cpp_sources` before generating bindings. Since the function is declared in `cpp_sources`, pybind11 can bind it. The actual definition is in `cuda_sources`, which gets linked in. Wait, but auto-generated pybind11 code uses the function name directly. So the forward declaration in `cpp_sources` is necessary. Let me structure it this way. Actually, I just checked some real examples. Here's a pattern from PyTorch docs and issues: ```python module = torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline( name='my_module', cpp_sources=''' #include <torch/extension.h> torch::Tensor my_func(torch::Tensor x); ''', cuda_sources=''' #include <torch/extension.h> __global__ void my_kernel(...) { ... } torch::Tensor my_func(torch::Tensor x) { my_kernel<<<...>>>(...); return x; } ''', functions=['my_func'], ) ``` This should work perfectly. Is there any issue with shared memory bank conflicts? For `gid = k * hidden_size + tid` with hidden_size = 256 and 32 banks: - Bank = gid % 32 = (k * 256 + tid) % 32 = tid % 32 - So threads in a warp access banks tid % 32, which are all different. No bank conflicts! For k=0: bank = tid % 32 For k=1: bank = (256 + tid) % 32 = tid % 32 Same banks, but different addresses. Still no bank conflicts because each thread accesses a different bank. Actually, shared memory has 32 banks. If thread 0 accesses bank 0 at address 0, and thread 1 accesses bank 1 at address 1, etc., there's no conflict. But if thread 0 accesses bank 0 at address 0 and thread 32 accesses bank 0 at address 256 (256 % 32 = 0), they'd conflict if they're in the same warp. But thread 32 is in warp 1, not warp 0. So warp 0 and warp 1 don't execute simultaneously in a way that causes bank conflicts (each warp executes independently). Actually, on modern GPUs, warps within the same thread block can be active simultaneously if there are enough resources. But bank conflicts only happen within a warp, not across warps. So no bank conflicts. Wait, that's not quite right. Bank conflicts happen when threads in the SAME warp access the same bank. Threads in different warps don't cause bank conflicts with each other because they don't execute the same instruction simultaneously in the same cycle. Actually, on Ampere and later, there might be some bank conflict checking across different sub-warps, but generally bank conflicts are intra-warp. So our shared memory access pattern is bank-conflict-free. Great. Now let me think about whether I should optimize further before trying to compile. Probably not - let me just write it and iterate. Here's my final code: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline CPP_SOURCE = r''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <vector> torch::Tensor lstm_forward_cuda( torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor h0, torch::Tensor c0, std::vector<torch::Tensor> w_ih_list, std::vector<torch::Tensor> w_hh_list, std::vector<torch::Tensor> b_ih_list, std::vector<torch::Tensor> b_hh_list ); ''' CUDA_SOURCE = r''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <vector> __device__ __forceinline__ float sigmoid_custom(float x) { return 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-x)); } __global__ void lstm_forward_kernel( const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ h0, const float* __restrict__ c0, float* __restrict__ c_out, const int64_t* __restrict__ w_ih_ptrs_int, const int64_t* __restrict__ w_hh_ptrs_int, const int64_t* __restrict__ b_ih_ptrs_int, const int64_t* __restrict__ b_hh_ptrs_int, int batch_size, int seq_len, int input_size, int hidden_size, int num_layers ) { int batch_idx = blockIdx.x; if (batch_idx >= batch_size) return; int tid = threadIdx.x; int num_threads = blockDim.x; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* h_s = smem; float* c_s = h_s + num_layers * hidden_size; float* gates = c_s + num_layers * hidden_size; float* input_s = gates + 4 * hidden_size; // Load initial h and c for (int layer = 0; layer < num_layers; layer++) { int loff = layer * hidden_size; int goff = layer * batch_size * hidden_size + batch_idx * hidden_size; for (int i = tid; i < hidden_size; i += num_threads) { h_s[loff + i] = h0[goff + i]; c_s[loff + i] = c0[goff + i]; } } __syncthreads(); for (int t = 0; t < seq_len; t++) { // Load input for (int i = tid; i < input_size; i += num_threads) { input_s[i] = x[batch_idx * seq_len * input_size + t * input_size + i]; } __syncthreads(); int cur_in_size = input_size; for (int layer = 0; layer < num_layers; layer++) { const float* w_ih = (const float*)w_ih_ptrs_int[layer]; const float* w_hh = (const float*)w_hh_ptrs_int[layer]; const float* b_ih = (const float*)b_ih_ptrs_int[layer]; const float* b_hh = (const float*)b_hh_ptrs_int[layer]; // Initialize gates with bias for (int i = tid; i < 4 * hidden_size; i += num_threads) { gates[i] = b_ih[i] + b_hh[i]; } __syncthreads(); // W_ih * input for (int j = 0; j < cur_in_size; j++) { float x_val = input_s[j]; #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < 4; k++) { int gid = k * hidden_size + tid; gates[gid] += w_ih[j * 4 * hidden_size + gid] * x_val; } } __syncthreads(); // W_hh * h for (int j = 0; j < hidden_size; j++) { float h_val = h_s[layer * hidden_size + j]; #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < 4; k++) { int gid = k * hidden_size + tid; gates[gid] += w_hh[j * 4 * hidden_size + gid] * h_val; } } __syncthreads(); // Apply activations float* h_layer = h_s + layer * hidden_size; float* c_layer = c_s + layer * hidden_size; for (int i = tid; i < hidden_size; i += num_threads) { float gi = sigmoid_custom(gates[i]); float gf = sigmoid_custom(gates[hidden_size + i]); float gg = tanhf(gates[2 * hidden_size + i]); float go = sigmoid_custom(gates[3 * hidden_size + i]); float c_new = gf * c_layer[i] + gi * gg; float h_new = go * tanhf(c_new); c_layer[i] = c_new; h_layer[i] = h_new; if (layer < num_layers - 1) { input_s[i] = h_new; However, we also need to handle the reduction for matrix multiplications. Matrix multiplication: `W_ih (4H, I) * x (I)` where I=128 or H=256. - If we have 256 threads per block, each thread can compute one element of the 4*H vector. But W_ih has 4*H=1024 rows. So with 256 threads, each thread could compute 4 elements (1024 / 256 = 4). So each thread handles 4 gate elements across the 4 gates. Alternatively, we can compute with 1024 threads, but that might exceed block limit or cause low occupancy. 256 threads per block is good. Let's think about how to compute `y = W * x`: - W shape: [1024, I] (col-major if transposed). - If W is transposed (so shape [I, 1024]), then y[j] = sum_i x[i] * W_t[i, j]. If each thread handles `j`, then each thread computes the dot product of `x` and a column of W. - With I=128, each thread does 128 multiplications and 128 adds. That's fine. - With I=256 (for higher layers), each thread does 256 multiplications and 256 adds. So if we transpose W_ih to shape [in_size, 4*hidden_size] (i.e., [I, 1024]), each thread can compute its assigned output(s) by iterating over `i`. **Gate layout**: 4 gates * hidden_size = 1024 elements. Let's assign 4 elements per thread (256 threads). For a given thread `tid` (0..255): - It computes gate indices: `tid`, `tid+256`, `tid+512`, `tid+768`. - It loops over `j=0..in_size-1`: - Load x[j] (shared). - For each of the 4 indices, fetch W_t[j, index] and multiply-add. Similarly for W_hh (shape [hidden_size, 4*hidden_size]), loop over `j=0..hidden_size-1`: - Load h[j] (shared). - For each of 4 gate indices, fetch W_hh_t[j, index] and multiply-add. **Data in shared memory**: - H states per layer: 6 layers * 256 = 1536 floats. - C states per layer: 6 * 256 = 1536 floats. - x input: max 256 floats. - gates: 1024 floats. Total shared memory: 1536 + 1536 + 256 + 1024 = 4352 floats = ~17KB. That's fine. We need enough for each block. H100 SM has 228KB. We can fit many blocks per SM. Actually, with batch_size=10, we have 10 blocks. Occupancy will be great. **SIMD efficiency**: If each thread reads W_t[j, *] for 4 consecutive elements? If W_t is stored as [in_size, 4H], then the 4 elements for thread `tid` are at offsets `j*4H + tid`, `+ tid+H`, `+ tid+2H`, `+ tid+3H`. These are strided by H=256 floats = 1024 bytes. On H100, global memory load for 4 separate 4-byte loads might not be fully coalesced if each thread accesses 4 non-contiguous locations. But even if we load 4 floats per thread, the warp (32 threads) will load 32 floats from one contiguous segment, then another segment offset by H*4 bytes, etc. That could be okay but not optimal. Alternative: reorder weights so that the 4 gates are interleaved? Like gate i is at offset tid*4 + gate_id. Then threads access contiguous memory. But standard LSTM weight layout is [i,f,g,o] stacked, each of size H. PyTorch's lstm weights are [4*H, I] with i=0..H-1, f=H..2H-1, etc. We can permute the weights during model initialization to [H, 4, I] or [H, 4]. But that changes the numerical layout if we just do `t()`. Actually, we can just store the transposed weights and let each thread load 4 consecutive floats if we reorder. Let's see. If weights are stored as [H, 4, I] (so contiguous for each hidden unit's 4 input connections), then thread `tid` (mapping to hidden unit `tid`) can access `w[j, 0..3]` where j is the input dim index. Wait, that would mean W_ih shape [I, H, 4]. For a fixed input j, hidden unit h, and gate g, the element is at [j, h, g]. Then thread `tid` (h) accesses [j, h, 0], [j, h, 1], [j, h, 2], [j, h, 3], which are contiguous (4 floats). The warp would access 32 * 4 = 128 contiguous floats — very coalesced. To do that, for each layer's W_ih (shape [4H, I]): - Reshape to [4, H, I] - Permute to [H, 4, I] (or transpose differently) - But actually we need `w[j, h, g]` so that for each input j, the gate weights for all h are contiguous in groups of 4. We can lay it out as [I, H*4] where for each input j, the block for hidden h starts at offset h*4 and contains 4 floats for gates. Let's formalize: Original W_ih (gate major, input minor): shape [4*H, I]. We want `w_interm` shape [I, H, 4]. This can be done by: - `w.T` -> [I, 4*H] - `w.T.reshape(I, 4, H).permute(0, 2, 1)` -> [I, H, 4] - Flatten last two dims: [I, H*4] Then thread `tid` computes hidden index `h = tid`. It needs to access elements for this h at all j. For a given j, the starting index in the flattened weight is `j * H * 4 + h * 4`. Then it reads 4 floats: gates 0,1,2,3. Same for W_hh: original [4*H, H]. We want layout [H, H*4] where for prev_hidden j, the block for current h is at `j * H * 4 + h * 4`. So we can prepare these "interleaved" weights in the constructor. Then in the kernel, each thread `tid` (mapping to hidden unit `tid`) computes: ``` float sum0 = bias[g * hidden_size + tid] // wait, bias is [4*H], need same reordering. ``` We also need bias in the same interleaved format: shape [H, 4] or [4*H] with (h, g) interleaved: `bias[h*4 + g]`. Instead of `b_ih[i] + b_hh[i]`, we initialize 4 sums from bias_interleaved[tid*4 + 0..3]. So `bias_interleaved` is length [4*H] reshaped as `reshape(H, 4).contiguous().view(-1)`. Similarly for W_ih_interleaved and W_hh_interleaved. This data layout will give us much better memory coalescing and potentially allow vector loads (float4). **Kernel design v2**: ``` // Shared mem: // h_states: [num_layers * hidden_size] // c_states: [num_layers * hidden_size] // input_s: [max(input_size, hidden_size)] // gates_s: [hidden_size * 4] (optional, but we can store per-thread in registers) // Per-thread registers: // float gate_sum[4]; ``` For each time step t: // Load x[t] into input_s // Loop over layers: // layer_in = input_s (if layer 0) or h_states[prev_layer] (can reuse input_s by copying) // Compute gate sums: // Initialize gate_sum[0..3] = bias[layer][tid * 4 + g] // For j in 0..layer_in_size-1: // x_val = layer_in[j] // w_ptr = &w_ih[layer][j * hidden_size * 4 + tid * 4] // #pragma unroll // for g in 0..3: gate_sum[g] += x_val * w_ptr[g] // For j in 0..hidden_size-1: // h_val = h_states[layer * hidden_size + j] // w_ptr = &w_hh[layer][j * hidden_size * 4 + tid * 4] // for g in 0..3: gate_sum[g] += h_val * w_ptr[g] // Apply activations: // i = sigmoid(gate_sum[0]) // f = sigmoid(gate_sum[1]) // g_ = tanh(gate_sum[2]) // o = sigmoid(gate_sum[3]) // c_new = f * c_old + i * g_ // h_new = o * tanh(c_new) // c_states[layer * hidden_size + tid] = c_new // h_states[layer * hidden_size + tid] = h_new // if layer < num_layers-1: input_s[tid] = h_new (need __syncthreads before next layer uses it) ``` Important: `h_states` and `c_states` need to be in shared memory because threads need to read all `h` values for the next layer (layer_in) or for the recurrent connections across `j`. The matrix multiplication loops over j from 0 to hidden_size, and each thread reads h_states[layer*H + j]. For the input to layer>0, the previous layer's output is placed into `input_s`. Since we have hidden_size=256 and input_size=128, we allocate `input_s` of size `max(input_size, hidden_size) = hidden_size`. **Memory layout for weights**: Since we need to pass weights per layer, we could stack them in `__init__`: ``` self.w_ih = nn.Parameter(torch.stack([...], dim=0)) # shape [num_layers, I, H*4] or [num_layers, H, I*4] depending... ``` But wait, the input size per layer changes.
