The C++ function signature can be: ``` torch::Tensor lstm_forward_cuda( torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor h0, torch::Tensor c0, torch::Tensor weight_ih, // [num_layers, max_in, 4*H] but with actual sizes? ... ) ``` Actually the easiest is to define 4 tensors `w_ih_all`, `w_hh_all`, `b_ih_all`, `b_hh_all` that are just concatenations along dimension 0 (or another dimension), plus an `int Tensor` for offsets. For example: - `w_ih_all` is a 2D tensor of shape [total_in_rows, 4*H]? Not quite, rows per layer differ. - Let's store `w_ih_all` as a 1D flattened tensor. We also need `w_ih_offsets` (length num_layers+1) to know where each layer starts. That's easy. In __init__: ``` w_ih_parts = [] offsets_ih = [0] for layer in range(num_layers): w = self.lstm.weight_ih_l[layer] # [4H, in_size] # process to interleaved transposed w_t = w.t() # [in_size, 4H] w_parts.append(w_t.reshape(in_size, H, 4).permute(0, 2, 1).reshape(in_size, 4*H).contiguous()) offsets_ih.append(offsets_ih[-1] + in_size * 4 * H) self.register_buffer('w_ih_all', torch.cat(w_ih_parts, dim=0).contiguous()) # shape [sum_in, 4H] self.register_buffer('w_ih_offsets', torch.tensor(offsets_ih, dtype=torch.int32)) ``` Actually `permute(0, 2, 1)` on `[in_size, 4, H]` gives `[in_size, H, 4]`. Then `reshape(in_size, 4*H)` flattens H and 4 together. So the resulting tensor has shape `[in_size, 4*H]` where the columns are grouped as (h0_g0, h0_g1, h0_g2, h0_g3, h1_g0, ...). This is exactly what we need. Then the offset for layer `l` inside `w_ih_all` is `w_ih_offsets[l] * 4 * H`. But because it's a 2D tensor, we can compute pointer offset as `w_ih_all.data_ptr<float>() + w_ih_offsets[l] * 4 * H`. Actually, `offsets` is in number of rows (each row has `4*H` elements). So row offset * `4*H` gives element offset. Similarly for `w_hh_all`: all layers have same in_size=H. ``` w_hh_parts = [] offsets_hh = [0] for layer... w = self.lstm.weight_hh_l[layer] # [4H, H] w_t = w.t() # [H, 4H] w_interleaved = w_t.reshape(H, H, 4).permute(0, 2, 1).reshape(H, 4*H).contiguous() w_hh_parts.append(w_interleaved) offsets_hh.append(offsets_hh[-1] + H * 4 * H) ``` Wait, the shape of w_t is [H, 4H]. `reshape(H, H, 4)` doesn't make sense because the second dimension is 4H, which is H * 4, so we can reshape [H, H, 4] by splitting the last dimension. That gives [H, H, 4]; permute(0, 2, 1) gives [H, 4, H]; then `reshape(H, 4*H)`. Let's check: Original w_t: [prev_H, 4*H] = [H, 4*H]. `w_t.view(H, 4, H)` yields grouping of gates across hidden units? Actually `view(H, 4, H)` splits 4H into (4, H). Then permute(0, 2, 1) -> [H, H, 4]. Then `reshape(H, H*4)` results in each row corresponding to a previous hidden unit j, and within that row, the columns are grouped by current hidden unit h and its 4 gates. That means for a given j, the offset for current hidden h is `h*4`. So w_ptr = &w_hh_all[layer_offset + j * H * 4 + h * 4]. But wait, if we want `w_hh` to be accessed by thread `tid = h`, and for each j we load 4 consecutive floats, then the memory layout should be: for each j, for each h (0..H-1), the 4 gates are contiguous. So `[j, h, 4]` flattened to `[j, h*4 + g]`. That means the 2D shape is [H, H*4]. And the offset for j is `j * H * 4`. That matches the above. However, `w_t.reshape(H, 4, H).permute(0, 2, 1).reshape(H, H*4)` does exactly this: - Start: [H, 4*H]. - Reshape to [H, 4, H]: for each prev_h j, the columns are (gate0 for h=0..H-1, gate1 for h=0..H-1, ...)? No, `reshape(H, 4, H)` groups the last dimension `4*H` into `4 x H`. The ordering of elements in memory is linear. The original columns are `[i0..iH-1, f0..fH-1, g0..gH-1, o0..oH-1]`. Reshaping to `(4, H)` without changing order gives `[i0..iH-1]` as the first H elements, then f, etc. Then the dimensions are `(H(prev), 4(gate), H(curr))`. So for a fixed prev_h j, we have a 4xH block where rows are gates and cols are curr_h. That's not what we want. Wait: `w_t` shape [prev_H, 4*curr_H]. Memory layout: row-major. For each j (prev_H): elements = [W[j, 0], W[j, 1], ..., W[j, 4*H-1]] where W[j, g*H + h] corresponds to gate g, curr_h h. Now `reshape(H(prev), 4, H(curr))` gives: `view[j, g, h] = W[j, g*H + h]`. So for a fixed j, `view[j, :, :]` is [4, H] with gates as rows, curr_h as cols. Then `permute(0, 2, 1)` -> `[prev_H, curr_H, 4]`. So `perm[j, h, g] = W[j, g*H + h]`. Then `reshape(H(prev), curr_H*4)` -> for each j, a row of length H*4 where elements are `perm[j, h, g]` in order h=0..H-1, g=0..3. So for given j and h, the 4 floats are at offset `h*4 + g` within the row. Thus `w_hh_interleaved` is shape [H, H*4] and element (j, h*4+g) = original W_hh[g*H + h, j]. And access: `ptr = &w_hh_all[layer_off + j * H * 4 + h * 4]`. This is perfect. Now for W_ih: Original shape [4*H, I]. Transpose -> [I, 4*H]. `reshape(I, 4, H).permute(0, 2, 1).reshape(I, H*4)`. Result: for each input j, a row of length H*4 where elements are (h*4 + g) = original W_ih[g*H + h, j]. Access: `ptr = &w_ih_all[layer_off + j * H * 4 + h * 4]`. Great. **Bias packing**: Bias shape [4*H]. We want to interleave similarly: `[h*4 + g]` where h in 0..H-1, g in 0..3. Original bias: `b[g*H + h]`. We need `b_interleaved[h*4 + g] = b[g*H + h]`. We can create by `b.view(4, H).t().reshape(H*4)`. Because `view(4, H)` has rows as gates, cols as h. `.t()` -> [H, 4]. `.reshape(H*4)` -> contiguous in (h, g) order. So `b_ih_interleaved = b_ih_l.reshape(4, H).t().contiguous().view(-1)`. Similarly for `b_hh`. **Offsets**: We can precompute offsets and store as `int32` tensors. `w_ih_offsets`: shape [num_layers+1], cumulative number of rows. `w_hh_offsets`: shape [num_layers+1]. `b_ih_all`: shape [num_layers, H*4] (since all layers have same 4H). Actually we can stack them without offsets: [num_layers, H*4]. `b_hh_all`: same. This is much cleaner. The C++ function signature becomes: ``` torch::Tensor lstm_forward_cuda( torch::Tensor x, // [B, T, I] torch::Tensor h0, // [L, B, H] torch::Tensor c0, // [L, B, H] torch::Tensor w_ih_all, // [sum_I, 4H] transposed interleaved torch::Tensor w_hh_all, // [sum_H, 4H] transposed interleaved torch::Tensor b_ih_all, // [L, 4H] interleaved torch::Tensor b_hh_all, // [L, 4H] interleaved torch::Tensor w_ih_offsets, // [L+1] int32 torch::Tensor w_hh_offsets, // [L+1] int32 int input_size, int hidden_size, int num_layers ); ``` Then we don't need any CPU-to-GPU copies in forward, no `std::vector`, and no pointer arrays. This makes the C++ side extremely simple. The kernel parameters: ``` const float* x, const float* h0, const float* c0, float* c_out, const float* w_ih_all, const float* w_hh_all, const float* b_ih_all, const float* b_hh_all, const int* w_ih_offsets, const int* w_hh_offsets, int B, int T, int I, int H, int L ``` Inside the kernel, for layer l: ``` int w_ih_row_off = w_ih_offsets[l]; int w_hh_row_off = w_hh_offsets[l]; const float* w_ih_l = w_ih_all + w_ih_row_off * (4*H); const float* w_hh_l = w_hh_all + w_hh_row_off * (4*H); const float* b_ih_l = b_ih_all + l * (4*H); const float* b_hh_l = b_hh_all + l * (4*H); ``` And compute. **Kernel details**: - We must handle `num_layers=6`, `hidden_size=256`, `batch_size=10`. - Each block handles one batch instance. - `threads_per_block = hidden_size`? But hidden_size=256. 256 threads is nice. It matches warp size 32 (8 warps). - However, to improve occupancy and instruction-level parallelism, maybe using 128 threads with each handling 2 hidden units would also work. But 256 is fine. - Shared memory: `h_s[6*256]`, `c_s[6*256]`, `input_s[256]`, `gate_s` can be registers. Actually, `gate_s` need not be shared — we can store in registers per thread. Each thread handles one hidden unit. It computes 4 gates and keeps them in registers. Then it writes to `h_s` and `c_s`. Wait, if each thread handles 1 hidden unit, we have 256 threads. But `hidden_size` is always 256 in this problem. That's great. For layer 0, `input_size=128`. We need `input_s` of size 128. For other layers, input is previous `h` of size 256. We can just use `input_s` of size 256 and load accordingly. **Detailed kernel steps for each block (batch_idx)**: ``` __shared__ float h_s[MAX_L * MAX_H]; __shared__ float c_s[MAX_L * MAX_H]; __shared__ float input_s[MAX_H]; // max(I, H) int tid = threadIdx.x; int l = layer index; // Load h0 and c0 for (l=0; l<L; l++) { h_s[l*H+tid] = h0[l*B*H + batch_idx*H + tid]; c_s[l*H+tid] = c0[l*B*H + batch_idx*H + tid]; } __syncthreads(); for (int t=0; t<T; t++) { // Load input x[t] if (tid < I) input_s[tid] = x[batch_idx * T * I + t*I + tid]; // if I < H, the rest is unused for layer 0 __syncthreads(); for (int l=0; l<L; l++) { int in_size = (l==0) ? I : H; int w_ih_row_off = w_ih_offsets[l]; int w_hh_row_off = w_hh_offsets[l]; const float* w_ih_l = w_ih_all + (long long)w_ih_row_off * (4*H); const float* w_hh_l = w_hh_all + (long long)w_hh_row_off * (4*H); const float* b_ih_l = b_ih_all + l * (4*H); const float* b_hh_l = b_hh_all + l * (4*H); float g0 = b_ih_l[tid*4+0] + b_hh_l[tid*4+0]; float g1 = b_ih_l[tid*4+1] + b_hh_l[tid*4+1]; float g2 = b_ih_l[tid*4+2] + b_hh_l[tid*4+2]; float g3 = b_ih_l[tid*4+3] + b_hh_l[tid*4+3]; // W_ih * input for (int j=0; j<in_size; j++) { float xj = input_s[j]; int base = j * (4*H) + tid * 4; g0 += xj * w_ih_l[base + 0]; g1 += xj * w_ih_l[base + 1]; g2 += xj * w_ih_l[base + 2]; g3 += xj * w_ih_l[base + 3]; } // W_hh * h #pragma unroll for (int j=0; j<H; j++) { float hj = h_s[l*H + j]; int base = j * (4*H) + tid * 4; g0 += hj * w_hh_l[base + 0]; g1 += hj * w_hh_l[base + 1]; g2 += hj * w_hh_l[base + 2]; g3 += hj * w_hh_l[base + 3]; } float gi = 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-g0)); float gf = 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-g1)); float gg = tanhf(g2); float go = 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-g3)); float c_new = gf * c_s[l*H + tid] + gi * gg; float h_new = go * tanhf(c_new); __syncthreads(); // make sure all threads read c_s before writing? c_s[l*H + tid] = c_new; h_s[l*H + tid] = h_new; __syncthreads(); if (l < L-1) { input_s[tid] = h_new; } // __syncthreads() before next layer reads input_s __syncthreads(); } } // Write final c states for (l=0; l<L; l++) { c_out[l*B*H + batch_idx*H + tid] = c_s[l*H + tid]; } ``` **Shared memory sync issues**: We need to be careful with `__syncthreads()` to avoid race conditions. The pattern above: - After computing `h_new`, we write to `h_s[l*H+tid]` and `c_s[l*H+tid]`. Then `__syncthreads()`. - If next layer uses `input_s`, and `l < L-1`, we set `input_s[tid] = h_new` either before or after the syncthreads. Since all threads write to their own `tid` in `input_s`, and all threads read `input_s[j]` for all j in the next layer, we need a `__syncthreads()` after writing `input_s` and before reading it. So putting `__syncthreads()` after `input_s[tid] = h_new` is correct. - Then at the start of the next layer, we compute gates using `input_s[j]`, which is safe because of the syncthreads. - Also, when we read `h_s[l*H + j]` in the recurrent loop, we are reading the same layer's h. We need to ensure that previous time step's writes to `h_s` are visible. Since the time step loop starts fresh, and at the end of previous time step we had `__syncthreads()`, so at the start of the next time step, all threads see updated `h_s` and `c_s`. One concern: `h_s` is updated at the end of layer loop. For the next time step, all threads read `h_s[0*H + j]` as the new h for layer0. Since at the end of time step t-1, all threads did `__syncthreads()`, reading is safe. But wait, after the final `__syncthreads()` of time step t-1, the next iteration's `// Load input x[t]` only touches `input_s`. Then inside `l=0`, we read `input_s` and `h_s[0*H+j]`. Since `h_s` was written at the end of t-1 and syncthreads, it's fine. What about `c_s` read during computation? `float c_new = gf * c_s[l*H + tid] + gi * gg;`. Here each thread reads its own `c_s`. No cross-thread read of `c_s` is needed. So it's safe. However, we need to make sure `c_s` is not being written by other threads at the same time. Each thread only writes its own `c_s`. So no hazard. But for memory consistency within the block, after `c_s` was written by other threads in previous time step or layer, the reads are safe after `__syncthreads()`. In our loop, after a layer writes `c_s`, we call `__syncthreads()` before moving to next layer. So when layer l+1 reads `c_s[(l+1)*H + tid]`, it's reading its own cell state that was written at the end of previous time step or initialized at start. That's safe. Wait, but each thread reads `c_s[l*H + tid]` (its own). If another thread wrote to `c_s[m*H + j]` for m != l or j != tid, that's irrelevant. So no need for syncthreads specifically for `c_s` reading. But we still need syncthreads for `input_s` and `h_s` cross-thread reads. **Potential performance bottleneck**: The inner loops for `W_ih * input` and `W_hh * h` iterate over `j`. For each `j`, all threads read the same `input_s[j]` or `h_s[l*H + j]` (broadcast). Then they read 4 floats each from global memory. Since threads in a warp read contiguous `base + 0..3` across threads? Let's see: `base = j * 4*H + tid * 4`. For warp threads 0..31, addresses are `j*4H + 0*4`, `j*4H + 1*4`, ..., `j*4H + 31*4`. These are 128 contiguous bytes. This is perfectly coalesced! The hardware can load a 128-byte cache line per warp. Excellent. After loading `x_val` (which is broadcast via shared memory or constant cache), we do 4 FMAs per thread. So arithmetic intensity: 4 loads + 4 FMAs. The loads are coalesced, so we should be memory-bound if the weights don't fit in cache. Let's calculate weight size: - W_hh per layer: 256 * 1024 floats = 1,048,576 bytes = 1 MB. - 6 layers = 6 MB. - W_ih total: ~1.4 MB. Total ~7.5 MB. This easily fits in L2 cache on H100 (50 MB). In fact, it may even fit in L1 or L1.5. So after the first few accesses, weights should be cached, making the kernel compute-bound or at least not heavily memory-bound. The total compute per sequence: - For each t (512), each layer (6), each j: - W_ih: in_size FMAs per gate. Layer0: 128 * 4 = 512 FMAs per thread. Other layers: 256 * 4 = 1024 FMAs per thread. - W_hh: 256 * 4 = 1024 FMAs per thread. - So per time step: layer0: 512 + 1024 = 1536 FMAs. Layers1-5: 1024 + 1024 = 2048 FMAs. - Total FMAs per thread: 512 * ((1536 + 5*2048)) = 512 * (1536 + 10240) = 512 * 11776 = 6,029,312 FMAs. - For 256 threads per block and 10 blocks: 256 * 10 = 2560 threads total. - Total FMAs: 2560 * 6M = ~15.4 GFLOPS (actually FLOPs, not FMAs; each FMA is 2 FLOPs? Usually FMA counts as 2). So about 30 GFLOPs. H100 does 67 TFLOPS FP32, so theoretical min time is ~0.45 microseconds. But realistically, with overhead, maybe a few hundred microseconds. Wait, PyTorch LSTM cuDNN is likely heavily optimized, but it also does unnecessary work (computes output tensor for all time steps). However, it's probably using tensor cores or very optimized pipelines. Our kernel might not beat cuDNN's highly tuned implementation unless we avoid a lot of overhead. But maybe cuDNN for this small batch and hidden size doesn't use tensor cores and has some overhead. Custom kernels can sometimes outperform cuDNN for small batches because cuDNN is optimized for large batches with persistent algorithms. Also, our kernel avoids computing the full output tensor (B,T,H) and storing it. cuDNN might have to allocate and write the full output buffer. Avoiding that could save significant memory bandwidth. Additionally, cuDNN usually computes `out` for all layers, but PyTorch's nn.LSTM with `batch_first=True` runs the entire sequence. The output returned is [B, T, H]. We skip that. This could be beneficial. However, we also skip the `self.fc` layer, but that was dead code anyway. Let me also consider using `__half`? The problem says "produce numerically equivalent outputs at fp32 precision." That means we need to run in fp32 and match fp32. We can't use fp16 for the main computation. That reduces instruction count. ``` float4 w_ih_4 = reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(w_ih_l + base)[0]; g0 += xj * w_ih_4.x; ... ``` This requires `base` to be aligned to 16 bytes. Since `4*H = 1024` is divisible by 16 floats? Wait, 4 floats = 16 bytes. `base = j * 1024 + tid * 4`. `tid*4` is a multiple of 4 floats, but is it a multiple of 16 bytes? `tid * 4 * 4 = tid * 16` bytes. Yes, for integer `tid`, `tid*16` is a multiple of 16 bytes. And `j*1024*4 = j*4096` is also multiple of 16 bytes. So `base` is aligned to 16 bytes. Great. That's fine. **C++ wrapper**: Now the C++ wrapper becomes very simple: ``` torch::Tensor lstm_forward_cuda( torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor h0, torch::Tensor c0, torch::Tensor w_ih_all, torch::Tensor w_hh_all, torch::Tensor b_ih_all, torch::Tensor b_hh_all, torch::Tensor w_ih_offsets, torch::Tensor w_hh_offsets, int input_size, int hidden_size, int num_layers ) { int B = x.size(0); int T = x.size(1); // int I = input_size; // int H = hidden_size; // int L = num_layers; auto c_out = torch::empty_like(c0); int threads = hidden_size; int blocks = B; size_t smem_size = (num_layers * hidden_size * 2 + hidden_size) * sizeof(float); lstm_forward_kernel<<<blocks, threads, smem_size>>>( x.data_ptr<float>(), h0.data_ptr<float>(), c0.data_ptr<float>(), c_out.data_ptr<float>(), w_ih_all.data_ptr<float>(), w_hh_all.data_ptr<float>(), b_ih_all.data_ptr<float>(), b_hh_all.data_ptr<float>(), w_ih_offsets.data_ptr<int>(), w_hh_offsets.data_ptr<int>(), B, T, input_size, hidden_size, num_layers ); return c_out; } ``` But `x.size(1)` is the sequence length. The test only checks forward. **Numerical equivalence**: One important detail: PyTorch's LSTM implementation includes bias in a specific way. The LSTM cell computes: ``` i = sigmoid(W_ii @ x + b_ii + W_hi @ h + b_hi) f = sigmoid(W_if @ x + b_if + W_hf @ h + b_hf) g = tanh(W_ig @ x + b_ig + W_hg @ h + b_hg) o = sigmoid(W_io @ x + b_io + W_ho @ h + b_ho) c' = f * c + i * g h' = o * tanh(c') ``` Where bias is added twice (ih and hh). That's exactly what we do. Another detail: PyTorch's LSTM has `bias_ih_l` and `bias_hh_l`. If `bias=False`, these don't exist. But the default `nn.LSTM` has `bias=True`. The reference didn't pass `bias=False`, so it's True. We can safely assume biases exist. **Checking correctness**: After implementing, run correctness. Since these are known only at runtime, we can pass `smem_size` to the kernel and allocate `extern __shared__ float smem[];`. **Code structure**: CUDA kernel: ``` __global__ void lstm_forward_kernel( const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ h0, const float* __restrict__ c0, float* __restrict__ c_out, const float* __restrict__ w_ih_all, const float* __restrict__ w_hh_all, const float* __restrict__ b_ih_all, const float* __restrict__ b_hh_all, const int* __restrict__ w_ih_offsets, const int* __restrict__ w_hh_offsets, int B, int T, int I, int H, int L) { extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* h_s = smem; float* c_s = h_s + L * H; float* input_s = c_s + L * H; int batch_idx = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; // Load initial h and c for (int h = tid; h < L * H; h += blockDim.x) { int loff = batch_idx * L * H + h; h_s[h] = h0[loff]; c_s[h] = c0[loff]; } // Note: no need to zero input_s __syncthreads(); for (int t = 0; t < T; t++) { // Load input for (int i = tid; i < I; i += blockDim.x) { input_s[i] = x[batch_idx * T * I + t * I + i]; } __syncthreads(); for (int l = 0; l < L; l++) { int in_size = (l == 0) ? I : H; long long w_ih_off = (long long)w_ih_offsets[l] * (4LL * H); long long w_hh_off = (long long)w_hh_offsets[l] * (4LL * H); const float* w_ih_l = w_ih_all + w_ih_off; const float* w_hh_l = w_hh_all + w_hh_off; const float* b_ih_l = b_ih_all + l * (4 * H); const float* b_hh_l = b_hh_all + l * (4 * H); for (int h = tid; h < H; h += blockDim.x) { float4 b_ih4 = reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(b_ih_l + h * 4)[0]; float4 b_hh4 = reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(b_hh_l + h * 4)[0]; float g0 = b_ih4.x + b_hh4.x; float g1 = b_ih4.y + b_hh4.y; float g2 = b_ih4.z + b_hh4.z; float g3 = b_ih4.w + b_hh4.w; // W_ih * input for (int j = 0; j < in_size; j++) { float xj = input_s[j]; const float4* w_ptr = reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(w_ih_l + j * (4 * H) + h * 4); float4 wv = *w_ptr; g0 += xj * wv.x; g1 += xj * wv.y; g2 += xj * wv.z; g3 += xj * wv.w; } // W_hh * h #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < H; j++) { float hj = h_s[l * H + j]; const float4* w_ptr = reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(w_hh_l + j * (4 * H) + h * 4); float4 wv = *w_ptr; g0 += hj * wv.x; g1 += hj * wv.y; g2 += hj * wv.z; g3 += hj * wv.w; } float gi = 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-g0)); float gf = 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-g1)); float gg = tanhf(g2); float go = 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-g3)); float c_old = c_s[l * H + h]; float c_new = gf * c_old + gi * gg; float h_new = go * tanhf(c_new); c_s[l * H + h] = c_new; h_s[l * H + h] = h_new; if (l < L - 1) { input_s[h] = h_new; // write after all threads done? Need syncthreads. } } __syncthreads(); // ensure h_s and c_s updated before next layer reads } } for (int h = tid; h < L * H; h += blockDim.x) { c_out[batch_idx * L * H + h] = c_s[h]; } } ``` Wait, there's a subtle issue: for `l < L-1`, every thread writes `input_s[h] = h_new`. But `h` iterates with stride `blockDim.x`. So thread tid writes `input_s[tid], input_s[tid+256], ...`. But the next layer needs to read all `input_s[j]` for `j=0..H-1`. Since all threads write to `input_s` across all `h`, and we have `__syncthreads()` at the end of the layer loop, it's safe. But do we need `__syncthreads()` between the `c_s` write and the next iteration's `h_s` read? The next iteration is `l+1`. The `l+1` layer loop starts with a `for (int h = tid...)` that reads `b_ih_l`, then `input_s[j]`. It doesn't read `h_s` or `c_s` for layer `l`. It reads `h_s` for layer `l+1` (recurrent connection) and `c_s` for layer `l+1`. But `h_s[l+1]` and `c_s[l+1]` were last updated in the previous time step (or initialized). They haven't been touched by layer `l`. So no hazard. However, at the end of time step `t`, the last layer writes `c_s[(L-1)*H+h]` and `h_s[(L-1)*H+h]`. Then there is `__syncthreads()`. Then next time step `t+1` starts. All threads read `input_s` (which is overwritten for layer0). For layer0, the `W_ih * input` loop reads `input_s[j]`. The `W_hh * h` loop reads `h_s[0*H + j]`, which was written by layer0 in the previous time step. Since the previous time step ended with `__syncthreads()`, reading is safe. But wait, in the current code, at the end of each layer loop, there is `__syncthreads()`. However, inside the layer loop, there is a `for (int h = tid; h < H; h += blockDim.x)` loop that threads may finish at different times (e.g., if H is not a multiple of blockDim.x, but for H=256 and blockDim.x=256, it's single iteration and all threads finish at the same time. So `__syncthreads()` is safe. If H is smaller than blockDim.x, some threads do 0 iterations, but they still reach `__syncthreads()`. `__syncthreads()` requires all threads in the block to reach it. If some threads take a different path (e.g., `if (h < H) { ... }` and then `__syncthreads()` outside the if), they all reach it because the `__syncthreads()` is after the for loop, not inside. The `for` loop condition handles inactive threads naturally. Wait, if H=128 and blockDim.x=256, threads with tid=128..255 execute `for (h=tid; h<128; h+=256)` zero times. They skip the body. Then they reach the `__syncthreads()` after the for loop. That's safe because all threads in the block reach the sync point. Good. Let me rewrite. ```cpp // Host wrapper torch::Tensor lstm_forward_cuda( torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor h0, torch::Tensor c0, std::vector<torch::Tensor> w_ih_list, std::vector<torch::Tensor> w_hh_list, std::vector<torch::Tensor> b_ih_list, std::vector<torch::Tensor> b_hh_list, int input_size, int hidden_size, int num_layers) { int B = x.size(0); int T = x.size(1); auto c_out = torch::empty_like(c0); std::vector<int64_t> w_ih_ptrs(num_layers); std::vector<int64_t> w_hh_ptrs(num_layers); std::vector<int64_t> b_ih_ptrs(num_layers); std::vector<int64_t> b_hh_ptrs(num_layers); for (int i = 0; i < num_layers; i++) { w_ih_ptrs[i] = reinterpret_cast<int64_t>(w_ih_list[i].data_ptr<float>()); ... } auto opts = torch::TensorOptions().dtype(torch::kInt64).device(x.device()); auto w_ih_dev = torch::from_blob(w_ih_ptrs.data(), {num_layers}, opts).clone(); // or torch::tensor but that creates CPU then copies. Better create on CPU then to device. auto w_ih_cpu = torch::tensor(w_ih_ptrs); // CPU int64 auto w_ih_dev = w_ih_cpu.to(x.device()); // Similarly for others... kernel<<<...>>>(..., w_ih_dev.data_ptr<int64_t>(), ...) return c_out; } ``` Wait, `torch::from_blob` with a CPU pointer and then `.clone()` to GPU might also work. Or just: ```cpp auto w_ih_dev = torch::empty({num_layers}, opts); auto accessor = w_ih_dev.accessor<int64_t, 1>(); for (int i=0; i<num_layers; i++) accessor[i] = w_ih_ptrs[i]; ``` This avoids CPU tensor creation. In the Python `forward`: ```python w_ih_list = [] for layer in range(self.num_layers): w = getattr(self.lstm, f'weight_ih_l{layer}').t().contiguous() w_ih_list.append(w) ... ``` This is clean and correct. Now for the kernel, I'll write it to work with the original (non-interleaved) weight layout, to avoid any weight transformation issues. This means the inner loop computes each gate separately. Wait, the reference PyTorch LSTM weights are: `weight_ih_l[k]`: shape `(4*hidden_size, input_size)` for layer k=0, or `(4*hidden_size, hidden_size)` for k>0. The gates are ordered: i, f, g, o. If I use the transpose, it becomes `(input_size, 4*hidden_size)`, and columns are [i0, i1, ..., f0, f1, ..., g0, g1, ..., o0, o1, ...]. To compute gate g for hidden unit h: ```cpp gi = sum_j x[j] * w_ih[j, h] gf = sum_j x[j] * w_ih[j, hidden_size + h] gg = sum_j x[j] * w_ih[j, 2*hidden_size + h] go = sum_j x[j] * w_ih[j, 3*hidden_size + h] ``` I'll keep it this way to avoid any interleaving bugs. Kernel: ```cpp __global__ void lstm_forward_kernel( const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ h0, const float* __restrict__ c0, float* __restrict__ c_out, const int64_t* __restrict__ w_ih_ptrs, const int64_t* __restrict__ w_hh_ptrs, const int64_t* __restrict__ b_ih_ptrs, const int64_t* __restrict__ b_hh_ptrs, int B, int T, int I, int H, int L) { extern __shared__ float sbuf[]; float* h_s = sbuf; float* c_s = h_s + L * H; float* x_sh = c_s + L * H; int batch = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; for (int i = tid; i < L * H; i += blockDim.x) { h_s[i] = h0[batch * L * H + i]; c_s[i] = c0[batch * L * H + i]; } __syncthreads(); for (int t = 0; t < T; ++t) { for (int i = tid; i < I; i += blockDim.x) { x_sh[i] = x[batch * T * I + t * I + i]; } __syncthreads(); for (int l = 0; l < L; ++l) { int in_size = (l == 0) ? I : H; const float* w_ih = (const float*)w_ih_ptrs[l]; const float* w_hh = (const float*)w_hh_ptrs[l]; const float* b_ih = (const float*)b_ih_ptrs[l]; const float* b_hh = (const float*)b_hh_ptrs[l]; for (int h = tid; h < H; h += blockDim.x) { float gi = b_ih[h] + b_hh[h]; float gf = b_ih[H + h] + b_hh[H + h]; float gg = b_ih[2 * H + h] + b_hh[2 * H + h]; float go = b_ih[3 * H + h] + b_hh[3 * H + h]; for (int j = 0; j < in_size; ++j) { float xv = x_sh[j]; gi += xv * w_ih[j * (4 * H) + h]; gf += xv * w_ih[j * (4 * H) + H + h]; gg += xv * w_ih[j * (4 * H) + 2 * H + h]; go += xv * w_ih[j * (4 * H) + 3 * H + h]; } for (int j = 0; j < H; ++j) { float hv = h_s[l * H + j]; gi += hv * w_hh[j * (4 * H) + h]; gf += hv * w_hh[j * (4 * H) + H + h]; gg += hv * w_hh[j * (4 * H) + 2 * H + h]; go += hv * w_hh[j * (4 * H) + 3 * H + h]; } float i_gate = 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-gi)); float f_gate = 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-gf)); float c_tilde = tanhf(gg); float o_gate = 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-go)); int ch = l * H + h; float c_new = f_gate * c_s[ch] + i_gate * c_tilde; float h_new = o_gate * tanhf(c_new); c_s[ch] = c_new; h_s[ch] = h_new; if (l < L - 1) x_sh[h] = h_new; } __syncthreads(); } } for (int i = tid; i < L * H; i += blockDim.x) { c_out[batch * L * H + i] = c_s[i]; } } ``` And host wrapper receives vectors, builds pointer tensors on the device, and launches. Now, to handle the linker issue, I can try setting `os.environ['CXX']` before `load_inline`. Let me try in the Python code. Actually, I'll just try one last compile with everything included and see if it works. Wait, I already know the evaluation harness is triggering the linker issue. But perhaps if I don't use any extra compiler flags, the default behavior works. Actually, in my first attempt I used `extra_cuda_cflags=['-O3']`. In the later ones I used various flags. Let me try without ANY extra flags. But more importantly, the host compiler might be the issue. Setting `os.environ['CXX'] = '/usr/bin/g++'` explicitly (or to the conda one) might help. Another potential issue: LSTM in PyTorch might use a different activation for the new candidate cell. In PyTorch, the formula is: i_t = sigmoid(W_ii x_t + b_ii + W_hi h_{t-1} + b_hi) f_t = sigmoid(W_if x_t + b_if + W_hf h_{t-1} + b_hf) g_t = tanh(W_ig x_t + b_ig + W_hg h_{t-1} + b_hg) -- Wait, in PyTorch, the gates are input, forget, cell, output. The cell gate uses tanh. o_t = sigmoid(W_io x_t + b_io + W_ho h_{t-1} + b_ho) c_t = f_t * c_{t-1} + i_t * g_t h_t = o_t * tanh(c_t) Yes, g_t uses tanh. In my kernel I used `tanhf(gg)` where `gg = W_ig*x + W_hg*h + b_ig + b_hg`. Correct. So the formula is correct. Let me now write the code. I also want to avoid the `c_out` tensor issue: I'm creating `c_out = torch::empty_like(c0)` on the same device as c0. c0 is passed from Python and should be on the correct device. For the pointer packing: ```cpp std::vector<int64_t> wih(L), whh(L), bih(L), bhh(L); for (int i=0; i<L; i++) { wih[i] = reinterpret_cast<int64_t>(w_ih_list[i].data_ptr<float>()); whh[i] = reinterpret_cast<int64_t>(w_hh_list[i].data_ptr<float>()); bih[i] = reinterpret_cast<int64_t>(b_ih_list[i].data_ptr<float>()); bhh[i] = reinterpret_cast<int64_t>(b_hh_list[i].data_ptr<float>()); } auto dev = x.device(); auto wih_t = torch::empty({L}, torch::TensorOptions().dtype(torch::kInt64).device(dev)); ... ``` Actually, I can create CPU tensors and move them. ```cpp auto wih_cpu = torch::from_blob(wih.data(), {L}, torch::kInt64); auto wih_dev = wih_cpu.to(dev); ``` But `from_blob` doesn't own the memory and might go out of scope if not cloned. Actually, `.to(dev)` clones it, so the temporary wih_cpu lifetime is fine. Let me just use `torch::tensor` on CPU. ```cpp auto wih_dev = torch::tensor(wih, torch::TensorOptions().dtype(torch::kInt64).device(dev)); ``` This should work. Actually, in libtorch, `torch::tensor` from a vector creates a CPU tensor. When we specify `.device(dev)`, if dev is CUDA, it will copy to CUDA. So yes: ```cpp auto wih_dev = torch::tensor(wih, torch::TensorOptions().dtype(torch::kInt64).device(x.device())); ``` This is clean. Now putting it all together. Setting env vars inside the exec'd module is fine, it modifies the current process environment. Now, for the CUDA kernel itself, I want to ensure correctness of memory access. Each block handles one batch. `blockDim.x` can be e.g. 256. For the first layer input size = 128, for others = 256. Shared memory: - h_s: L * H = 6 * 256 = 1536 floats - c_s: 1536 floats - x_sh: max(I, H) = 256 floats Total = 3328 floats * 4 bytes = 13.3 KB. Well within limits. Threads = 256. Since H=256, one warp per hidden unit? No, 256 threads / 32 = 8 warps. Each warp processes 8 hidden units in lockstep for the j loops. Performance should be decent. Let me try to compile this.
